RATING: 9
- The game can be rather opaque in understanding the long term ramifications of something, especially sub 4-5 plays of it. After almost 20 plays, I still regularly both stumble through (plus lose), and learn something new. Not a game that supports a mixed experience group well without seriously getting the newer players up to speed with the analysis required (which is woefully difficult to do effectively). It's a game of incentive/partnership management with a serious meta component that evolves based on the group. Second, it's a game of pacing, some strategies (like FCM) will depend on a shorter game, where as some depend on a longer one. Understanding how to read the game pace is of a massive benefit and central to the game. Controlling that pacing is key, and good luck doing so in your first series of games. Reading who is going to help push that pacing is both important and difficult. I actually think that's one of the most difficult tasks; especially since it's controlled by the auctions/actions/plans/desires of the rest of your field. Last, it's a game of position management; "sometimes it's better to ensure you don't make a buck (or 2) rather than my coffers to fill."


Tweaks lots of things that I find enjoyable. My worry is related to the first point; once we get a core group who are experienced with it, how effectively will other players be (and thus, how much enjoyment will I be able to squeeze out of the game when there are new players)? In particular, how will having someone who isn't reading the game state in the game effect the ability to accurately predict that pace and what sort of chaos will that introduce? Extrapolating from other games would tell me that you still achieve some enjoyment, but it's much more long term marriage, not dating passion. Some games fade exponentially then, some don't, and (much like the butterfly effect here) I'm not sure where this falls yet. 


25+ games in, I'm not sure I buy that the auction action is *hands down* the most effective action you can take. I agree that it's the most *dangerous* action, but not necessarily an auto action as I've seen some folks make it out to be. For that, I think it depends upon the group. I've seen people auction every chance they get and still place last because they are in a group of people who don't value the auction. Conversely, I've seen people use it sparingly, but everyone does and it becomes much more potent. Proper valuation and speed of paper movement is also a group-dependent factor; if everyone wants to drag it out, paper is more valuable, but if some value the auction and some value the paper, then even though the game may end in 5 rounds, the paper has a real chance of winning (thus, the commitment starvation game is tougher to accomplish). A different group which built/dev'd less would make it happen more often, which brings me back to the core point; it's an emergent game of groupthink, and controlling the pacing means having to understand how the rest of the players view the game. If this game took 90-120 minutes, I don't think I'd rate it a 9, but at an hour or less, it's jam packed.

RATING: 10
John Borher packed a ton of depth into a short game here, excellent all around, really an achievement in board game design. It's a nasty portfolio game where victory requires smart bidding, properly timing auctions, and wrecking other people's companies. The underlying mechanisms inevtitbaly lead to collusion, a dynamic and brittle landscape of  short-lived alliances and plenty of back stabbing, which all takes place in a 45 minute game -  brilliant!

RATING: 9
A game for connoisseurs of player interaction. Short, sweet, and shoots for the head.

Everything you do doesn't just affect you, it directly helps or hurts everyone else in the game. It ties you to other players' fortunes, mis- and otherwise. And there's no randomness.

If you find yourself shackled to someone when they make a mistake, sorry, but it sucks to be you. It's entirely possible for the newbie at the table to latch onto you and drag you down with them, and not have any idea that they've lost the game for themselves and others.

Once everyone knows what they are doing you'll find in Wabash Cannonball the best experience boardgames have to offer.

RATING: 7
A fairly simple network building game, with increased complexity due to the auctioning of shares in various companies (comparable in mechanics, although much lighter in complexity, to the 18XX series of train games). It's a competitive, meaty, strategic affair, with no luck involved - a really solid design, with nice production values.

RATING: 7
2 plays / 4,6 players (1 play Wabash version)

LIKE: Quick, simple turns, but deep.

DISLIKE: A game, at first, feels like a black box. Sometimes auctions involve knowing exactly how much to spend in an auction and when. Sometimes players could feel behind in the whole game. Experienced players have an edge. The early "Wabash" version felt bland, lacked theme.

OVERALL: I like it. It's a game where some subtleties are lost on me because you want to try to get someone else to invest in a line that you may have a better position on, especially on the turn or two after them. Buying a share also dilutes the company, which at times I'll forget. It felt very competitive.

RATING: 6
Basically should always be great fun. However, can turn into a rather flat experience if all players end up with identical portfolios. The only way to gain an edge over others is either to get the shares cheaper or diversify your portfolio from the competition. Once everyone has equal shares, no one has any incentive to waste actions on developing the companies. Hence, I think the game could benefit from some other ways to earn money besides the companies paying dividents.

After all these complaints, it is just fair to give the praise. The game looks fantastic, plays fast, and the basic rules are super easy to remember since they all have a real life correspondent. For a 60 minute game, Chicago Express is time well invested. Be it any longer, and it would certainly see no table time.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I'm pretty wowed by this one -- CE is top-notch gaming. It's easy to play, but the ability to play [i]well[/i] is going to require a lot of hands-on experience. Components-wise, the trains are cute, but I'd prefer the more austere Winsome-style cubes for manageability (I can't believe I'm saying that...).

RATING: 9
Mezcla genial entre juego de trenes y juego económico. En el aspecto ferroviario hace algunas abstracciones para que no tengas que preocuparte de cosas como el transporte de mercancias, aunque ofrece sensaciones similares a otros juegos de trenes más pesados. De esta forma el juego realmente depende del aspecto económico: las subastas; el modo en que obtienes y compartes compañías de otros jugadores; cómo haces alianzas temporales con ellos; la tesorería, los ingresos y las oportunidades de crecimiento de las compañías, etc.
Tiene una gran profundidad alcanzada a través de reglas sencillas, duración de la partida relativamente corta, hasta seis jugadores, y componentes expectaculares en la edición de Queen (aunque la mía es una versión casera).
-------------------------------------------------
Great mix between a train and an economic game. On the train side, it makes some abstractions so you don't have to deal with delivering goods for example, but it offers similar feelings as other heavier train games. So the game really relays on the economic side: the auctions; the way you acquire and share companies from other players; how you make temporary alliances with them; treasury, income and company development opportunities, and so on.
Main features are deep game through easy rules, short playing time, playable up to six players and really superb components on the Queen edition (although mine is a homemade version).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A light train game.  The one I've played so far was a 5-player game having 4 new people (including myself).

I don't like that the track indicates the overall value of the train line (e.g., instead of stock price per share).  This means that more owners/investors = a sharp dilution of earnings.  This also means that expected return on investment is significantly lower for train lines with more shares (since the four lines each have 3, 4, 5 or 6 shares).  For this reason alone, red > yellow (imo, of course).

I also dislike how diversity can equal winning.  As in someone can benefit greatly from free-riding from another player's managing of a train line.  Yes, that's part of the game, but if two players cooperate for one train line, or if one owner/investor fails to assist with a train line, it can significantly affect the success of that line.

In addition, I dislike how the mid-to-endgame becomes very obvious in terms of return on investment.  It seemed like further purchasing of additional shares would not be of benefit since the player would not earn back more than the cost.

Even in view of the above, I would like to play this one again.  I'm not sure whether or not my experience and conclusions are well-founded or accurate.  I could be way off.  I'm willing to give this another shot.  It's a nice lighter train game and doesn't take long.  I do like the action mechanic (the dials).

RATING: 9
1st play: I wasn't sure what to expect with this but the system was fun.  Trying to speculate on the future growth of the companies and the ability to buy into a company just to send the trains off in the wrong direction is an interesting system.  I'm not a huge fan of the train theme, but the game is great.  The production is good and the actions players take match the theme perfectly.  

2nd play: Wow, I knew I liked this game, but playing it again, I liked it even more.  There is a TON of game packed into 45 minutes, complete with auctions, route building, and opportunity to punish players who are overconfident in their position.  Manipulating round timing and ensuring you don't overinvest in a stock are very key to success in this game.  I haven't added the expansions yet, but I think they will add to the experience.  This is an example of a simple system that gets out of the way of the players in just the right way.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
I'd rather play: CARCASSONNE (multiplayer investment)

Overall, I can see what the appeal is of this game, but really the number of mechanisms it needs to engineer (!) the dynamic is too many. 

Ultimately, you are trying to time things appropriately, but I think often the granularity of the decisions can be so dense you are really just trying to make a read on player tendencies as to whether you can get that share or not. That's fun, and skilful, but for mine other games just do this more elegantly and more quickly. It's certainly not something I want to even spend an hour on with so much opportunity for seat order and kingmaking to intervene.  

I played this a lot on iOS and I can't fathom how anyone could enjoy the constant calculations in real life. It must create a lot of downtime. 

Games like this make me think the triumph of modern boardgaming is how they can take the tenets of gaming institutions like Role-playing, Wargaming and Train-gaming and make their dynamics accessible. The busyness of the theme could be very appealling to those who need  a real-life context, but I'm very happy with abstraction.

RATING: 10
[i]2017/05/20 3 plays 3-4-5 players[/i]
Bumped from 9 to 10. This is the best gateway 45 minute 3-5p game I have.

Tip : to reduce money handling, [url=https://boardgamegeek.com/thread/1783257/tip-reduce-money-handling]use the superfluous locomotive token in the home town to track rail budget on the dividend track[/url].

[i]2017/04/28 1 play 3p[/i]
Very elegant game with a limited set of rules but huge depth. Players compete for shareholding of train companies while extending rail networks and developing towns and the land.

Combined shareholding creates temporary alliances, but if one allows la large majority to emerge, the minority shareholder may poison the company.

Very interactive game, thanks to the share bidding auctions and spatial competition. Lot's of screwage and unexpected moves.

The game plays at a fast clip, and because of the internal timer of game end conditions, is finished within one hour, while creating a great play experience.

A great entry to shareholding games, playable with non-gamers.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The stock holding and railway expansion elements are nicely balanced and impressively streamlined. The Queen edition components are fantastic (minus money) and dials are a really 'neat' way of managing dividends and rounds. 

There's a surprising amount of room for some deep play  in what remains a fairly simple and quick game of this type. Honestly, one of my only real issues is that the game seems almost *too* short. Another issue is the map/company starting points. The setup doesn't really have anything modular about it and the railway goals (going west) are fairly linear. That being said, I still really like the dynamics of CE. It's a fantastic product.

Note: I think that the expansion Narrow Gauge and possibly Erie is needed to retain my full rating (without going down) at the 5-6 player count. There just doesn't seem to be enough options for clever play mid game if you make a misstep or get stuck in a corner. 3-4 seems like there's enough room for manipulation without necessarily adding expansion options.

(Own Queen Edition + Expansion)

RATING: 7
2-6(4)

2p worked, but I think it would be much more captivating with a third (at least) to muddle the ownership pool and make things a bit less zero-sum. Still, the decisions are pretty engrossing: you want to dilute their ownership and force them to overpay to maintain control of their own company, but if you put their company up for auction you might be using your action to fund them! Or, alternatively, trying to bait your opponent into taking a portion of a company you already have several shares of in order to use their overbid to fund a company you mostly own.

Also great is that it bucks most auction games in that you're not purely trying to get the lowest bid. Sometimes you, crucially, want to overbid to get your company income high enough (indeed, if you cut-rate bid your own stock all the time you might have a critically underfunded company!). I feel the game may run a bit too long if people evade auctions too much, but still a good one.

RATING: 8
A highly distilled railway game, full of tough decisions.  Chicago Express engages from the opening auction, through a desperate rush for the limited actions each turn, to the closing dividend. It's really interesting how share purchases affect both the company's ability to extend its network and how its dividend will be divided.  It feels as though margins for error are slight. There is plenty of scope for players to cooperate and to block or send competing lines in costly and unhelpful directions. Definitely a game with a learning curve.


Queen Games has transformed the pug ugly Winsome edition into a handsome game.  My only quibble is with the paper money (in not very useful 1-5-25 dollar denominations too).

RATING: 9
Extraordinary game ! 

With a few simple elements, 3 action tracks of different length, 5 railroad companies each with a different number of shares and a board made of hexagons of 3 types of terrain and 2 types of cities, the creator of this game has set up a "game world" of complex interwoven relationships between the players and the environment they live in for the duration of the game. 

These relationships give the players a fascinating situation to analyze before they make a decision and choose an action.

Although they are very different games I must say that I didn't have such pleasure while discovering a game since Puerto Rico.

Bravo to Harry Wu, Winsome Games and to Queen Games to have made this game widely available !

RATING: 10
Easy to teach and even the people not very enamoured with board games like it, and will discuss strategy after the game! This game is brilliant ... BRILLIANT!

The short play time, the multitude of decisions, the wonderful board and components, and the economy and route planning all make a winner. Do yourself a favour and try it.

I lost my first 4 games playing/teaching this, but still enjoyed it. Last game I wasn't teaching and I decided to be creative and let go of my inhibitions. I didn't try to prevent others from obtaining equal shares in companies in which I was the majority shareholder. When I was ahead on dividends I withheld on the urge to buy more shares. I waited for others to make moves. It becomes an almost existential experience. A slight pull here and a push there - controlling other's actions whilst concealing your own. Needless to say, I won - more than convincingly ... fantastic.

Latest session (5 people), I taught the game again, and got beaten again. Same story, everyone loved it and would like to play it again... my wife now knows most of my strategic thinking , having been present for most games - she is going to be tough to beat for awhile.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.75
Clever, and an entirely different train game experience, coming from the world of Ticket To Ride and Age of Steam. I like it, though it has a bit of a Puerto Rico feel to it in terms of figuring out when the game is about to end.

Definitely brutal, but not punishing in the way that one feels like they made a little mistake at the beginning and now they're hosed; feels more like a euro train game, with high-end elements of each.

That it can be played in about an hour is a huge bonus, since most of the high-thought games I have don't fall into this category.

Each time I play it I like it more, every game seems to end differently; had a 2-player game that I thought would be boring because of the auctions, and it turned out to be amazing -- it exposed a completely different strategy that also worked quite wheel (and beat me soundly). Wonderful game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This game is quite the interesting economic/stock market game. You invest in rail companies that are competing to get to Chicago, and they pay out dividends split among all of their stock shareholders. Selling additional stock of a company can bring additional players into the fold, but also dilutes the payout of individual shares.

Two concerns with it:
1) As Matt said, this needs to be played with poker chips instead of paper money.

2) I'm worried that there is an optimal strategy and runaway leader issue. Once you've got a particularly profitable rail, if nobody works towards giving your color of stock to someone else, you WILL win.

Regardless, I'd love to play it again.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Maybe the trick is not to let someone get too far ahead in the first place.  We allowed a player to get all 3 PA shares early,  and with his decent initial income, he stomped us.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is one of those games that I have a difficult time rating.  For such a simple rule set, it's astonished how deep players can dive into this game.  It's a game where every decision matters and one where players need to be vividly aware about the other players, their capabilities, and goals. It's also a game defined by shared incentives and emerging alliances.

That being said, this is a very opaque game that is difficult for new players to value.  And while I consider myself a fairly accomplished gamer, I still have a difficult time valuing decisions in this game.  And that's where the game becomes problematic; if players don't know how to value the decisions in the game, the decisions feel random and lackluster.  And perhaps worst, their uninformed decisions will inevitably effect the positions of other players at the table (king making). 

This is a gem that simply needs the right set of players.  And if players are new to this game, it may not be a bad idea to go over strategy/tactics.

RATING: 8
Now that Queens is going to print the game, hopefully with better graphics and components, I am now looking forward to purchasing the game for myself.

Jan-10-2009
First game with 5 other players.  The game is very cool.  Very unique and different from the rest of my collection.  The game is so simple to learn but there are so many things that you need to take into account to win.  I really like that you have alliances that form because you share stocks with other players and it is actually very beneficial to have alliances since you only have one action to help your company grow.  With other players being in the same company they have a lot of reasons to grow the company and helping you at the same time.  At the same time trying to time it just right to get that extra share in the company so that they are helping you more than themselves. It is really cool how the game just flips upside down so fast on you.  Playing a 6player game felt pretty contrived though.  You do not have as much control since 5 other people gets to go in between your turns.  There are also not enough shares to go around until the game ends.  I think in 6p games the only way to win is to get stocks from each company so you are in cahoots with everyone.  I think I'd have to add some variants for a 6p game to be more interesting.  I still liked the game with 6p, but I can certainly see why the game would be better with 4.  I re-read the rules and missed 4 critical game changing rules, for the better.  I can't wait for the next game.

RATING: 7
When the ruleset is simple and elegant, and the game is surprisingly deep, you know you are in front of a very good game. The fast playtime is definitely a bonus. Chicago Express, at its chore, is a game about construction of incentives for other players. It is extremely opaque, and the very nature of the game (every decision has cascade effects on every player) makes it a strange beast to ride: I suspect that very often the victory is handed to a player by a (very difficult to evaluate) mistake of another player.
Needs more plays to fully appreciate. Too bad it seems too chaotic with 5+ players, and doesn't work with just 2...

RATING: 8
[i]Posted: 4/5/11, Rated: 8[/i]

It's confession time again.  I own [i][thing=421]1830[/thing][/i], but I don't feel that my understanding of its genre is sophisticated enough to appreciate it.  [i][thing=94]Union Pacific[/thing][/i] is more my style.  [i]Chicago Express[/i] appears to be the "missing link" when it comes to train games: it has just enough share auction and corporate valuation to be intriguing, without a lot of rules surrounding it.  In fact, I think the gameplay has been smartly pruned down to the bare essentials for a game of this type.

Side note: some people say a rule set is "elegant," others say it is "pared down."  Either way, you have a game whose rules are simple to understand, yet encompass a surprising depth of play.  [i]Chicago Express[/i] is one of those games.

I love the artwork of the board, and the wooden train markers.  The company status boards look sharp, and I appreciate their being double-sided to support an individual preference for organization (I prefer the side with $1, $5, and $25 spaces).  This is a game I definitely want to climb back aboard!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.3
This game is absolutely brilliant.  I really enjoy seeing how poeple interact each and every game.  About fifteen minutes from the end of the game, it is clear that one or two players are not in the running for winning the game.  This is common with many games, and I feel that fifteen minutes is not long to be out of the running.  They become pawns of the leaders since they are not going to win, still must take turns, and still have influence through thier owned stock.  It is really funny to see what happens when the these people out of the running begin making meaningful choices that impact the players in the running.  I have never played a game with a similar negotiation element, which makes the game is fantastic.

Chicago Express es el juego económico que más me gusta con diferencia junto al Imperial. Creo que es una buena opción y un buena buena pieza en cualquier colección que se precie.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9

---------------------------------------------------------
Quick-ish rules explanation:
This is an amazing stock train game where 4/5(black) trains race to make a connection from the east coast back to Chicago. The game starts with an auction, one of each train stock, not counting black which may come out later. On a players turn they can take one of four actions;
1.)Auction a share - just auction a share up for bid. The winning bid is paid directly to that companies coffers.  Players can start an auction and pass if they are just devaluing a train. Theoretically everyone can pass and the stock gets pitched, but I don't see that ever happening.
2.)Build a house - Build a house for free on the board, increasing the value of that tile for the connecting or eventually connecting trains.
3.) Build trains - Pay money from the train's personal supply to build across the board, increasing stock value along the way. To build from a train you must own at least one share in the company. Only one train can be in forest and mountain tiles, but other tiles can be shared for more $$$. Reaching Chicago gives a big, one time payout and the first time it's reached it starts an auction for the black train.
4.)Pass - Moving one dial one spot forward and not taking an action.
The board itself shows each of the actions, along with gauges on the board linked to each. When you preform each auction you must move the gauge up one on the appropriate dial. When a dial red lines you can no longer take the action that round. When two dials red line the round is over, trains pay share holders and the dials reset.
The game ends if one of these conditions are met;
1.)Detroit's value hits 8 (Detroit moves up one after each round.)(Never seen it happen)
2.)You are down to three houses left in the supply.(Think the lowest we've seen is 8.)
3.)Three train companies run out of trains.
4.)Three companies run out of stocks.
The round is then finished and money is counted. Richest player wins.
---------------------------------------------------------
Well, now that I've made this game seem fairly boring and simplistic, let me assure you that this game is in fact possibly the greatest game ever created. Yes, actions are simple, but every play changes the outcome of the game. Projected profits of companies can be derailed. Sudden jumps toward Chicago can make trains significantly more valuable. It's amazing, and it's all player drive. No deck of cards being randomly draw, telling you what is happening for apparently no reason. The reason in this game is found by looking directly into the tiny beady eyes of the monsters you are playing.

I'm sure this game stinks with no table talk. People just keep to themselves, no taking of trains before they reach Chicago. For the most part, I wouldn't know because all I do is table talk. I want to know if you will be willing to suddenly run the red train south through the mountains crushing the blue and yellow train(Each company only has a certain amount of trains, so slight deviations from the path to Chicago will make the trip impossible.) I will try to convince you that allowing Blue to survive will put your grandma's house in danger of foreclosure. Any leverage I can get I'll use to my advantage in swaying people. There is no shame in my game, because when you beat your friends in this, you know you beat them because there was no luck involved.

I honestly can't say enough about the greatness of this game. It's difficult to cover all facets of what makes it so perfect. The closest I can come is the moment when you drive a train into Chicago right before a rival train connects, getting a huge cash boost and they suddenly realize you've made Chicago too expensive for their train to build into. Or when you abruptly send blue through the mountains and cut off a totally unaware opponents train, as they look helplessly at you just as you start twisting your mustache(fictitious or not.)

This game is a MUST BUY.

RATING: 9

May 2010:  I bumped it to a 9.  I have played with all numbers now and I think the game excells at 3-4 players.  Five and six, while fun and challenging, goes a bit too fast.  Also, the Narrow gauge and Erie have added a ton to the game.  I can't see playing without those.

RATING: 6.5
Pretty fast rail-building and stock game.  Gameplay varies with the number of players since there are only 4 main railroad companies and each one has a different number of stock certificates (3,4,5 or 6).  Also, there are a limited number of wooden trains for each company and unless you build directly to Chicago (hence the Express part of the game title), you will run out of wooden trains and never reach Chicago.  It follows the typical rail/stock game mold with player money and company money, dividends, and the goal being to have the most personal money at the end.  Wrapping your head around the dividend values is a little tough at first but not too difficult.  Initial stock purchases are typically $12-18 each, which becomes company seed money.  There are tons (~5) ways the game could end so it's kind of tough to remember all of them.  The game has almost no luck aside from not knowing what other players will do so it's important to figure out what is a fair price for shares and figure out how to work efficiently with the limited number of wooden trains.

Others say: The original Wabash Cannonball only differs in the way that dividends are rounded.

#plays (W.Cannonball): ? (best guess is 1)
#plays (C.Express): ? (best guess is 2)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Can't make my mind up on this one.

Half of me feels you can have lost the game in the first round, and with more than 3 there is a real impact of kingmaking (either deliberate or accidental). The other half loves a game of depth that has such a quick playing time.

I like the player interaction and the stock mechanic where shares dilute the holdings.

You need at least one partner (who's trying to HELP your company not screw it over) so you can get building routes fast, so you want equal sharing to give both incentives to help it, but if you have equal shares in a company then really you'd rather use your actions on a company where you have a monopoly, or majority.

Turn order is extremely critical. Many of the decisions you make should be driven by where you are in the seating order. If all the players realise this the game shines, if someone doesn't then they can hand the game to another without fully realising why ... :(

On balance I think the game needed a bit more development - shine has gone off, rating dropped and it's on the trade pile.

** Update, now traded - the half of me that feels the game is decided in the first round (although you might not realise it) or over influenced by kingmaking (possibly inadvertant) won out. Still consider playing it, just in no rush to.

RATING: 8
An 18xx game compromised and streamlined into 60 min, w/o the loss of game depth, a very solid design. Player interaction is really high here, as by your expanding actions you will help others. So the aim is to distribute the support or to be the one with the higher reward.
So it´s a very competive mutilayer game, with a degree of tension, player interaction and well calculated decisions.
The weak point is, that the game can be blown by players which don`t understand the nuances of the game. It´s a gamers game and should be never played with family (casual) gamers.

RATING: 7.5
A pesar de ser un juego de trenes, no hay que transportar nada. Simplemente hacer conexiones por las zonas más rentables. 
En juego hay cinco compañías y al mitad de juego entra una nueva. No pertenecen a ningun jugador en particular, sino que cada compañia tiene un numero de acciones que pueden salir a subasta. 
Las acciones de los jugadores pueden ser iniciar subastas, o hacer crecer la compañia (añadiendo vías, o estaciones). Interesante final de rondas (las rondas no siempre duran lo mismo, sino que va por unos contadores de acciones, muy original). 
El juego se acaba cuando se venden todas las acciones de tres acciones, una compaía se queda sin trenes o detroit (que es una especie de contador de rondas) llega a la ronda 8.  Cuando eso sucede gana el jugador con más pasta.
Es un  juego económico bastante redondo. Recomenable.

RATING: 7
Although this is ostensibly a train game, the map only seems to serve as a way to manipulate stock prices and track precedence in certain cities. 

At its heart, this is really an auction game, and a good one at that. If you're bidding for a stock you have heavily invested in, you may want to bid high, not only to keep your majority, but also to both increase your income and maintain enough money in the company to expand that train line later on. However, if you spend too much, you may leave another stock open for another player to snag at a very cheap rate. Often the timing of the auctions matters more than anything else, and managing your money is crucial, especially early in the game. 

Since I am not a huge fan of economic games, the "penny pinching" aspect of the game is not my favorite. On the other hand, the game has a very simple, elegant rule set, which makes it easy to learn (even though it can be maddeningly difficult to plan out a winning strategy with so many things to consider). All in all, it's a very good game. I'd like to try with more players.

RATING: 8
I like this game very much, even though I prefer to gut out the math rather than calculate everything.  Actually, that might be WHY I like the game.  Playing this with a bunch of accountants might suck all the fun out of it.

Where can I maximize my own cash while denying it to others?  This is the crucial question of Chicago Express, and since the answer changes every game - hell, it changes every TURN of every game - the game is compelling to play.  Plus, it's short, so you can play it twice in an hour or so (unless you're playing with the slow accountants, mentioned above).

Does it end too quickly?  Maybe.  But I suspect this game will be getting a fair amount of my gaming time in the coming year.

RATING: 6
UPDATE: Yeah, the replayability was pretty limited. At play 3, I'm about done, sadly. There just isn't as much game here as it initially seemed. It also seems like it's pretty much confined to a sweet spot of exactly 3 players. Extremely opaque and punishing to first-time players; moreso even than 1830, this is almost impossible to play satisfactorily with players of differing skill levels. But, I enjoyed a few games of it, it's an interesting game system, I'd certainly still play it again, but when the local game shop's semi-annual flea market came along, it was gone.
---
I seem to be getting generous in my old age, giving out a lot of 8s these days. Anyway, I thought Chicago Express was fun and quite clever. The mechanics of issuing and buying stock are rather unique in this whole stock-buying/train-building genre, so a lot of instincts are wrong (diluting your own shares by issues stocks in companies you own portions of and think are going to do well is frequently a bad idea, for example). The length of 60-90 minutes is perfect too.

I don't doubt that this will have rather limited replayability, like many of Queen's games. But also like many of their games, it's unique, clever, well-put-together, and very attractive game. Time will tell how well it will hold up, but for the moment, I'm optimistic.

All in all, lots of interesting decisions and tense moments as you see what moves your opponents make.  Great game!

RATING: 10
DISCLAIMER: I wrote the iPhone version of Chicago Express/Wabash Cannonball.

My favorite gamers' game.

Mechanically this game feels somewhat similar to Modern Art yet thematically it still feels like a train game. It narrowly beats Modern Art as my personal favorite auction game. The only disadvantages are 1) it's even more opaque than Modern Art making it tough for new players to grasp its nuances and, 2) again as with Modern Art, inexperienced players tend to randomize the winner. But I've realized this is true of any multiplayer game with any depth. Without this quality the game is either too much a multiplayer "solitaire" or its results are too dependent on player order.

A quick note on comparisons I've seen made to Diplomcacy. While it's true that Chicago Express revolves heavily around alliances, these alliances are not the product of negotiation. That is, they are not the result of cajoling, threatening, pleading, and begging as they are in negotiation games (Diplomacy, Intrigue, and sometimes Bohnanza for example). Rather, alliances are a natural consequence of players' current cash and share holdings. Alliances are "game theoretic", for want of a better phrase. Coca Lite discusses this idea at length (http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/315482). His posts, and those of J C Lawrence, are required reading for any new player.

RATING: 6

Update: I ended up trading for this game, playing it once, and trading it away. I had also played the ios app a bit. It's not a bad game. However, the difference between winning and losing between good players is miniscule... it's a game of eeking out that $1 or $2 advantage. And if someone is unfamiliar with the game... forget it. I respect the slick design, but I'd prefer more wiggle room in the game play.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
So far clearly the best of this year's Essen (2007) and I don't see anything that might challenge that position.  The obvious progenitors are Pampas Railways and Wooden Shoes and Iron Monsters.  From Pampas Railroads it borrows the same actions in the same distribution, special dividends and payout patterns, but loses the concentrated value of the development action due to the lower rate of sharing city locations.  From Wooden Shoes and Iron Monsters it borrows the core share dilution pattern and several flavours of end-game clock. but loses   the delicacy of turn order control and the layered aspect of the grouping shares.  It is game in which every decision counts, even the seemingly little ones.  

Wabash Cannonball is a sub-hour 3-4 player ultra-efficient knife-fight of an economic game.  I love it.  The primary patterns are emergent alliances and incentive structures.  Everything you do from the moment the game starts helps someone and hurts someone else, sometimes the same person.  Everything you do incentivises players to take actions that variously help and hinder you.  Much of the game comes down to ensuring that those short-lived emergent alliances, built totally on individual greedy self-interest, work in sum to your advantage.  Everything in the game, including how long the game will last and the rate at which the game will play, is in the player's control and driven by that mesh of incentives and self-interest that the players have communally built.  

This is a tough game.  Really, tough.  There is a lot of butterfly effect and the long term effect of decisions is often difficult to even vaguely estimate.  This results in a strange problem: While it is a game that greatly rewards skill, it is also particularly opaque.  This can often lead to players winning through no great skill of their own, but rather because the other players simply failed to prevent them from winning or unknowingly handed the win to another player.  This is not inherently a bad thing and many games are like that.  The problem is that because the game is so particularly opaque, that seeing that this is happening and preventing it presents an unusually subtle learning curve.  The result can leave many early games feeling somewhat random, and in truth they will be rather random...until the players start to catch on and all the players at the table are of comparable skill.    

What makes this particular trip so interesting is that the game is also highly prone to groupthink until the players really catch on to what is going on with the game.  Most of the groupthink is centred around the shifting alliances of cash and share ownership, and more so than most such games, the groupthink is so easily broken.  Most of our early games had the Pennsylvania reaching Chicago first and being a great cash cow.  The result is that the Pennsylvania shares are increasingly hotly contested -- all until the B&M and NYC both conspired, to their owner's mutual profit, to shut down Pennsylvania hard and fast before it had a chance to go anywhere.  The Pennsylvania players lost big in that game...and I see no reason for any groupthink to be similarly easily defeatable.  Another similar pattern: In another recent game the B&O was nigh to reaching Chicago first.  It seemed obvious that B&O would hit Chicago and flood money into the game.  The fourth and last B&O share was quickly auctioned and the player who already had two snapped it up.  The result was that the player with only one share in the B&O ran the B&O low on cubes so it couldn't reach Chicago, and then did the same thing to the NYC for similar reasons in the next round.  In a few easy actions he very efficiently removed two other players from contention, turning a four player game into a two player contest with 2 also-rans.  The resulting summary is that competition is good, but too much competition will lead to castration, which isn't so good...

Prepare for the game to end faster than you expect.  So far our fastest game was 3 players in 20 minutes.  Most games are under 45 minutes.  The longest was 4 players in 90 minutes including rules teaching.  Several 4 player games have ended in well under an hour including rules teaching.  Let the player to your right run your companies to Chicago.  Pick your partners carefully. Ensure that companies in which you are a minor investor can't reach Chicago due to cube or money exhaustion if you have something better to do.

Control of when the game will end is everything.  Be careful!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
:indigo: :( :what: :) :D :) :what: As tightly and beautifully designed as any game I've ever played. As many rules as are needed, with no more. As much complexity as needed, with no more. A tremendous amount of depth in a game that you can teach in 15 minutes and play in 45. 50% of the game is in the auctions, 45% in controlling the tempo of the game (and when to trigger the end), everything else is the frosting. Putting a share up for auction can several things at once: it can dilute the value of existing shares, it pumps money into a railroad, it can implicitly offer other players to join you in pushing a road you already own, it can implicitly sow division between the members of an existing partnership, and it means that someone is giving up a chunk of their hard-earned kitty, which is both their capital and their victory points.

Once you understand this, Chicago Express reveals its true identity as a nasty, filthy game of trust and betrayal. It takes a pretty extraordinary set of moves to run companies that make you enough money to win without help from [i]somebody[/i].

Agricola Syndrome is a problem for n00bs -- you'll set up a railroad that would absolutely win the whole damn thing for you if the game lasted twice as many turns as it did. But it doesn't, so it won't, so you just screwed yourself with your pants on, Giselle.

If a dominant strategy is emerging in your group, change up, it's groupthink. A good group who plays regularly will see fads emerge -- you'll go from "The Person Who Controls The PRR Controls The Universe" to "Why Would Anyone Buy Into This Broke-Ass Railroad" and from there to a more moderate opinion over time.

The only knock on this is stockholding games are not everyone's idea of fun, nor are auction games, so some people will not see it. But for the rest of us, it's as good as it gets.

Best with four players, but playable with 3 or 5.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.1
10-04-13 - Played one solo game to get the rules down.  This is a 90-minute railroad game that packs a real wollup of a punch.  This is an auction game for the ages.  While railroading might be the theme, you better your auction A-game to win this one.  The margins are tight, here, and the game will have to be played several times by players before they begin to grasp the order of actions, and when it makes sense to call up a share for an auction.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
9/1/08: The Erie expansion makes this game an easy 10.

Wow! This little firecracker of a game is dead simple, adorably presented, and terrific fun. Comparisons to the 18xx lines seem to be popping up all over the place. After playing the game, I don't really understand them. Wabash Cannonball is about as different from an 18xx as Age of Steam is.

A shocking amount of game is packed into a tiny little package. The relationship between players and corporations strongly encourages vicious collusion between players, while the rules for stock acquisition, corporate financing, and dividend 'break points' encourage careful, precise spending that should keep games close among alert players. Speculation is present, as route and income development can be foiled very quickly by a neighboring railroad that suddenly has a lot of shareholders eager to build some destructive track! Auctions should see profit margins sliced razor-thin.

More games, and the Wabash keeps getting more interesting. At first we didn't know what to do with it and thought its function was mostly do set the end of the game on a hair-trigger. Later, we began to see the value of running it directly to Chicago, especially if the round is almost over. Finally we are now learning to value the Wabash as a powerful later-game entity, with a fantastically challenging initial auction based on turn order, available actions, player cash, player position...

For such a challenging game, it's surprising how much control players have. That is to say: when a player discovers a new way to exploit the engine, he can usually make out like a bandit. However, the very next game (and sometimes even later in the same one!), the technique can be neutralized through collusion, or simple caution. The ease with which players can do this owes largely to the nakedness of the engine's pivot points and the large degree of control each individual has over the shape of a turn. This ease, in turn, generates a game where collusion is required but politics are a distraction. This is a rare treat and the best feature of this excellent title.

RATING: 8
I love that you can lose the game in the first few auctions and not realize it until the very end of the game.  There is much to explore here, and I am just scratching the surface.  the ruleset is easy to read and remember, but the gameplay is something else.  You need to understand the power (and necessity) of collusion.  You need to realize that the race to Chicago is not the meat of the game.  You need to collude to ruthlessly bring the leader back down and grind his railroads into dead ends in the mountains.  If not, you will probably have a boring game, and ask "is that it"?  It is almost like container in that respect.  The system can be fragile, but if your game is boring, I would place the blame on how the players made decisions. 

After a lot of soul searching, I have traded this great game away.  Although I found it immensely interesting, nobody I play with was ever that enamored with it.  In the end, it made more sense to trade it away for a game that might get played rather than let it sit on the shelf where I could longingly look at it from time to time.  I hope it lands with a good home.

RATING: 9
I've played twice.  Each game felt very different. I find that I really want to play again.

The components are entirely stark. The game really is nothing more than a prototype, components-wise. Also, I am not convinced that its really much of a simulation of anything. 

But none of that matters..because the game is genius fun. The turns are short. You can only do one of 3 actions (or take a non-action). There are limited shares of stock. There is limited track with which to build. 

The decisions become very much like a tug of war. Often, you can do little to improve your position on a turn, but you can hurt someone(s) else's.  Its important to recognize your strong choices and develop a bit of strategy. 

A weak player can probably inadvertently throw the game, so its important to play with fairly level skills among all players.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Wabash is essentially a refinement of the Prairie Railways Series with the math simplified, but with a few interesting nuances added into the mix. Players invest in companies by buying stock, that money is then used to expand routes and to increase the value of the railroads. Actions can also be expended to develop the areas that the railroads are connected to. The end goal is simple: make money.

So as I said, this game is strongly reminiscent of the Prairie Series, in particular, the best game in the series, Pampas. The action here is driven in the same way: by a very finite pool of actions that players compete for the use of. The refinements here are interesting and well implemented though, specifically the different amount of shares and track pieces available to each company as well as the way in which the stock is handled; as it dilutes easily, which creates evil player interaction, which I like.

Just like the Prairie games, this is a game where you want partners though, because then your company will get twice as many actions, and be more likely to get where you want to go. A company left by itself, with no work being done on it, and only the initial share sold can be quite profitable as well though, and players need to guard against that as well.

Also like the Prairie games, the turn order is extremely critical. Many of the decisions you make should be driven by where you are in the seating order. The Wabash is a great example of this, depending on where you are in the order when it opens and how many expansion and capitalization actions are left, it can either be extremely valuable, a way to make a buck or two, or a huge money and time sink that can/will cost you the game.

One question mark for me initially was the track building. The fact that anyone who owns a share can build track means that it is very easy, perhaps too easy, to burn up the company treasury in an aggressive move, and to exhaust valuable track tiles in the process. This creates an interesting tension to the game, and some unique interactions though, and has ended up being one of the benefits of the game with more experience, as it drives some very interesting auctions where a given person does not want another given person to win, because they know they are buying the stock just to trash their position.

The only notable blemish on the game for me at this point is the weakness of the development action's utility in comparison to the Prairie games. In the Prairie games, there are 6 companies instead of 4, and 5 stocks in each of them. What this means, in a game sense, is that there are more companies visiting each city, and each player can own more stocks over the course of the game. What this also means is that the development action, which functions similarly, is much, much more useful on average because there are more places where you have stocks in two or more railroads at the same locale, and hence, you can milk a bigger boost out of the action. The Pampas income calculation scheme also rewards companies for having multiple links into/out of a given locale rather than just being present [i]in[/i] that locale, so, for instance, having 4 links running into/out of a given area, and then developing that area leads to a quadruple bonus to the income...very strong. The Prairie games also let you double develop at a cost, which was inexplicably left out of Wabash as well, even further weakening this track. Given that Wabash is the first game in what will be a series, I anticipate this weakness in the game will be fixed either by increasing the number of rail companies, by allowing the double develop action, or by replacing it entirely. Any of those solutions, or all of them in concert, would likely improve the game system. When these changes occur, I anticipate Wabash's rating will fall. This isn't to say that development is completely useless, because it's not. In a longer game, it can add important value to company without having to expand track and the action can also be a good way to delay the end of the round (this second use is the one that it is most frequently put to).

All of this said, Wabash is a true heavyweight game, yet it is playable in less than an hour. It keeps the interesting alliance system from the Prairie games, but it changes the rules and interactions just enough to be in its own unique category. I think it's ultimately too limited to ever reach above 8 status and anticipate it falling to a 6-7 once revisions are made available (first coming at Esssen 2008). Hard to say though, since Wabash itself seems to be receiving expansions as well, perhaps one of those will push this game in the direction I would like it to go more than the descendant games will, hard to say at this point. 

The bottom line for the present though: if this game was longer it wouldn't be as good as it is, but with the short playtime and tough decisions, I have to respect this one.

Update, Fall 2008: I still really like Wabash but the rating for the base game has fallen to a 7. I wouldn't push it lower than that, but I always prefer to play the Erie version of the game which is at least one full notch better than the base game because of the decisions that it adds to the mix.

Update, Fall 2009: Wabash is slowly accumulating a stable of mini-expansions. I've now started ranking the game as a whole in this entry and then rating the expansions up or down from the base of 5 (average game experience) in their own entries. Wabash is a definite 9 at this point, though I think it's at its best with the mini-expansions (Erie, Narrow Gauge) thrown into the mix when you're playing with an experienced table, as both add some extra things to think about. It's important to take some time to play with each expansion though, as each adds some different texture to the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Chicago Express plays very rapidly, even with a full complement of 5, and has the benefit of stripping away nearly all unnecessary bagage which is weighing down other titles in this genre. As a result, the game's structure is exceedingly clear and understandable compared to other train & stock games, allowing the players to discover the ebb and flow of the game on their own without too much problems.

And that is more or less where their problems begin. Apart from a few gamey rules, this game is a complex exercise in n-party Nash equilibria where 2 ≤ n ≤ number of players. In normal language: it benefits a subgroup of players more to cooperate than to fight each other. In Chicago Express the typical pattern of this (usually seen between inexperienced gamers) is when two players divide the shares of one successful company between them, get paid big dividend, and then use that dividend to leverage themselves into other companies, diluting the market share of their respective owners. Once this pattern is up and running it is very difficult to derail it, and usually causes sour faces around the table. In order to counter this pattern, other players need to wrap their heads around a completely alien concept in euro-boardgaming: cooperation. Helping a competitor, expecting to be helped in return, but only to the point where the previously mentioned dynamic duo is reigned in; and all that because the alternative is [i]really[/i] to lose. And no cutting corners either. Players [i]need[/i] each other in order to survive, and that makes it both tough and interesting. Unfortunately to date I haven't played a single game which made the game 'work' as it should have.

Chicago Express makes it extra difficult for the other players because there is an 'entrance fee' of sorts to start cooperating, namely the price of purchasing stock. Other games based on this pattern like for example Die Dolmengötter and Bridges of Shangrila, do not have such an entrance fee, and are consequently more forgiving, and thus more fun to play. You may be behind the rest for a few moves, and still lose all the same, but at least there is a [i]feel[/i] of participation until the end. This has not been the case in any of the games of Chicago Express I have played to date. As a result I'm in two minds about this game. It does the Nash-equilibrium thing very well, and presents you with a tough challenge to boot. But at the same time the current rules create unpleasant game situations [i]very[/i] easily, giving it a masochistic learning curve. I cannot say if the level of self-punishment will subside once you become more experienced; as it stands now it's too much of an effort for me and the groups I play with. So I guess we're all not as hardcore as we thought.

Moral of this story: be careful with this game. Technically it is strong if not very strong, likely 8-material. Practically to date it has been average at best with drawn-out and unpleasant sessions, approximately a 5. I'm averaging to 6, but that doesn't tell the whole story [i]at all[/i].
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I like it.  Quite a lot.  Perfect information without seeming dry.  Auction psychology in a perfect information game is new to me, and adding to it the "dilution" factor lends to some interesting brinksmanship.  You may turn a profit by buying this... but will it get diluted further?  And, even if you do turn a profit, would you rather hold onto those dollars for the next auction?

The actual train mechanics are rather inconsequential.

RATING: 7
Components: Fun, detailed shares. Attractive board with mostly intelligible iconography.

Experience: Auction games are tough the first time around, especially when throwing in the math about how buying a new share will affect the price of shares, and estimating how many times dividends will be paid out by the end. Playing with secret money seems essential.

I like that players have just a simple choice on their turn, to take one of three actions. Extending train lines is fun, and it's clever both how they are funded from the railway's coffers, and how they increase the value of the line. I also like how player turns really influence downstream decisions; not only might my action close off a similar action for the next player, but it might change the value of remaining stock, thus making an auction more appealing. The game moves at a fast pace, and can end suddenly.

Last Played: 2011-09-13
Eagerness to Play Again: Within a year

RATING: 10
A great game.  The stock manipulation of 18XX without the playing time.  Even my friend who "Doesn't like train games" likes it.  Proper bidding in the auctions is the essential skill.  You don't want to bid too much, as the money will rot in the company treasury and you don't want to bid too little as the company won't have enough capital.  Also you want help building up the company, so you need partners.  Diplomacy is very important.  Wonderfully complex for such a short game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
Simple and elegant, with a short playing time, yet deep and thoughtful. There, that is Chicago Express in a nutshell.  It's a lovely game, and the Queen edition also looks great.  It's an auction game with a sort of area control mixed in.  But done a bit differently this time, with an absolutely brilliant idea of company's money being generated by the money paid by the winners in the share auctions themselves.  There really aren't many games around that pack such a punch in so short a time, so yes, this is a definite must in any serious collection.

RATING: 4.75
Chicago Express is a stock market train game following in the footsteps of the 18xx series, but simplified and made to play in 45 - 60 minutes.

I'm sure there is a good game in there, but it has been simplified so much that it is practically an abstract.  In fact, the low rating is because the theme in regard to how the stock market works made the mechanics more difficult to understand for me.  So the more familiar you are with how stocks work in the public markets, the more difficult it will be for you to wrap your head around this game. For instance, when someone else buys stock in your company, you lose a lot of value.  Normally this isn't the case since the value you lose is made up for by the money that is given to the company (a portion of which you own).

Once you get over the counter intuitive use of the stock market theme, then you find that the game is largely a timing game.  You want to manipulate when the game ends, because the only important factor is cash on hand at the end of the game (without regard for the value of the companies that you own).  This is counter intuitive because you can sell stock to get cash.  And if the companies are not public, then you wouldn't be able to buy them at will.  Anyhow, the theme is marginal at best, and it makes playing the game more difficult in more ways than it probably helps with understanding the game.

I can see the potential for this game, so my rating might go up in the future, but for now, I'm not eager to play again after my third try (and I didn't much enjoy the first two games).  It is simply too abstract and dry for me.  It feels like a math exercise where you're solving for X, and X is when the game will end.

--- Edit ---
I have now played this game enough to see that is has a good amount of depth for the small number of rules it uses.  I normally like games with few rules and a lot of depth, but this one doesn't work too well for me.  I don't like games where I have to work out mathematical calculations.  I much prefer games where it's not possible to figure out the math, so you have to sit back and make good judgement.  

If you like Powergrid and Age of Steam, you might like Chicago Express.  It's not my type of game.

RATING: 6
This is another train game where you buy stock in the railroad and it pays out based on how much revenue it generates.  It plays a lot quicker than 18XX and is much simpler.  I almost rated it an 8, but I'm not sure it has a lot of replayability.  There are several end-game conditions.  So I think the key strategy is managing how soon the game will end and adjusting strategy to take advantage of it.  My play was a 6er game, and there just wasn't enough stock to go around.  The only way to generate money is w/stock...and when 3 piles of stock are gone, the game is over.  So stock ran out quickly.  Luckily I had stopped buying after my initial 3 shares.

After another play, this game feels like it is lacking in depth. Dropping from 7--> 6.  Build out fast to chicago, hope others jump onboard to help you get there.

RATING: 8
(Chicago Express version).  Players are stock market players, investing in rail companies and influencing their capitalisation and expansion.  Very simple game where players get a chance to do (or do not) one of three actions, then wind the game clock forwards a cog.  Options are auction a new stock share, expand a railway company across the map, or improve an area of the map a company has already reached.  Auctions inject capital into companies, which is spent on expansion, which improves stock prices.  Every so often the game will pay dividends, paying out cash to the players.  Most cash wins.  Lovely looking game, plays fast, but boiled down, it's a very dry exercise in mathematics.  The oversized trains cover the board up fast, making the important details in a hex a pain to see.  It's like Knizia, Moon and Wallace got together and knocked out a train game.

RATING: 8
It's a no-luck (+) negotiation game (+) about trains (+). Every move shifts the incentives of other players in subtle (the development action) or far-reaching (auctioning a share in a powerful company) ways.

The potential downfall of a no-luck game is the tendency for games to fall into particular patterns. Will that occur with Chicago Express? That remains to be seen.

Played once with 2P, once with 3, once with 4, and an aborted play with 5. 2P will probably prove to be less interesting. That's OK, my girlfriend doesn't like it much anyway. I'm not actually sure if I'll be able to get this the play it deserves, but it certainly deserves play.

Edit: My group enjoys it, so it may get the play it deserves after all. Good with 3 and 4; I'm not entirely certain about 5, but we'll see with time. Player's shaping of the situation is incredibly important; know who you're playing with and what they're looking for.

And don't play with Eurogamers. The game is about making more money than everyone else, not making the most money!

RATING: 9
Impressive, fast, railway economic game with a lot to look at and consider throughout the game.   There's perfect information and some back-scratching.  Alliances are key, and timing of null actions can be critical.   It's subtle, but easy to learn.  The subtleties start to really shine with experienced players.  One of my favorite share-holding games.  I tend to prefer this over heavier, longer, more simulation-oriented train games.  There's a line where those become more work than game, but Chicago Express feels like 100% game to me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Now that Chicago Express has been played, I can compare it with Steel Driver which is a very similar game and fills a similar niche in one's collection.  Chicago Express feels like a more open game with more choices available.  Let's compare:  SD requires you to auction shares at the beginning of every round whereas CE gives the players the option.  SD requires that companies spend money they have if they are able to do so whereas CE allows you to build routes when you feel like or increase the companies by developing where they already have a route built.  SD ends in 5 rounds regardless of what happens whereas in CE there are multiple ways in which the game could end, but it still has a maximum number of turns.  I'm not even going to get into Steel Drivers end game cube grab again.  While Steel Driver is not a bad game, I can't see why anyone would rather play it over Chicago Express.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This game has one of the simplest rulesets around, but still delivers a real "train game" experience. I have played this with "gamers" as well as my family group with great success on both fronts. Any adult that has ever had to balance a check book or is capable of a tiny bit of math can pick this up, gamer or not.

Is this the best Gateway into 18xx games?

Found a possible flaw that keeps it from staying permanently in the Gateway category. The ability to really screw someone's available track moves the game into the cutthroat category. This is not something that will be apparent to new gamers, but it's hard not to take this action when you know it is optimal, but are still playing with new folks. It will seriously piss them off. 

Still a very enjoyable game!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A
Train game with auctions, more suited for gamers.  By Harry Wu.

Chicago Express is not for everyone, given that it’s an economic game that relies strongly on subtle interaction through auctions and tough decisions. But it has the advantage of being very accessible, and the simple rule-set and very quick play time makes it easy to get into and come back to. Those who are prepared to return to it will find themselves discovering more depth with each return visit, and a very deep and satisfying experience as a result of the changing alliances that lie at the heart of the game’s engine. The reimplementation by Queen Games has given high quality production values which will please gamers who appreciate the value of a tight and thoroughly rewarding game design that is as deep and replayable in view of its economics and interaction as it is accessible and beautiful. This is a highly regarded title well deserving of the many accolades it has received, and for new gamers to explore and discover the treasures it so readily offers.

Review: http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/818292

RATING: 7
Most of my plays of this one are via the iPhone app, not in person. That damn, Carnegie is tough to beat! A brilliant game for torquing the incentive grid while riding temporary emergent alliances to greater effect than your opponents. Er, can't remember, but I picked those terms up somewheres…

While I do think it's great at what it does, the experience is not in my wheelhouse of fun. It's not so much a marathon but rather a very short race in which you're cutting each other's limbs off and seeing who can crawl across the finish line. What I mean is, it's more about the opportunities of others that you destroy than it is about those you create for yourself. That's a perfectly fine Machiavellian experience, but not one I'll gravitate to first on game night. When I do wish to thwart, though, this is a great choice.

RATING: 7.3
Different enough from my other RR games.  It is an interesting & logical mechanic that money used to purchase shares is the money available for building--therefore, you want to buy shares at a high price, yet not so high that you can't purchase other shares.  You only spend your money on purchasing shares.  It is good to have the majority of a RR line, yet if another player is helping you build it (he must own shares in it), you have a better chance of being the first to Chicago, which yields bonuses. 

Nice board & components.

RATING: 7
Strangely similar to Steel Driver, but feels less abstract. It is therefore probably the friendlier of the two. Both are simple market games with hard-to-evaluate share values. In Chicago Express, the difficulty come not only from the possibility of reaching Chicago, but also from the potential for share dilution. This dilution mechanism is one of the more interesting aspect of the game, but still I slightly prefer Steel Driver for its simplicity.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Chicago Express is a short, rather meaty railroad game (of the stock market variety as opposed to pick-up-and-deliver). Since most actions benefit some players and hurt other (or even the same) players at the same time the best actions to take aren't always obvious. In the end, it seems the winner will simply be the one who manages to make the other players hurt him the least.

After the first game I'm not sure how much control one really has. There is no randomness as such in the game, but the direct consequences of the other players' choices mean that in some cases you're simply screwed. Once you have fallen behind the only way to come back is hoping that the other players hurt each other enough to let you squeeze back in.

Now, trying to manipulate those incentive structures so that other players leave you alone and harrass someone else instead is certainly interesting in its own right, and the game is short enough that this aspect doesn't weigh too heavily but it isn't entirely satisfactory, either.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
SUPER AWESOME 31st BIRTHDAY GIFT FROM CAITY & BRIAN!!

I've wanted a super decent train game for awhile and Chicago Express is that game. More so, it is a stock manipulation game that uses its mechanics in a way that no other game does.  Spending your money into the wealth of the company and then using that money to grow the company is a really interesting part of this game.  The balance that has to be found between your personal cash and how much you invest in stocks (even at a loss just to stop others from making too much) keeps the brain churning. For all the thinking that you have to do, this turns go by relatively quick that the game is very easy to understand once it begins.  I do find it very hard to cooperate with other players (which is often necessary) while still trying to prevent them from getting a lead. Overall: simple mechanics with huge outcomes. A real neat game. UPDATE: I find that the game has sort of worn on me. It's difficult to determine the repercussions of your actions without doing some math each time to figure out how stock get diluted; how much it affects the other players in comparison to you; if there is enough money being put into the companies, etc. It kind of takes away from the neat ideas in this game and puts too much focus on the raw, calculative pieces of it. It's a fine game and has some real smarts, but not a game to pull out only every now and then. I think this game is best for those people who play it repeatedly, and those just aren't my types of games these days. I like games you can get into quick and figure out while playing. Stock games always have the ability to be misunderstood by casual players as it's hard to gauge how much stocks are actually worth to you in the end.  Some games I can stand this, others I can't.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
3 or 4 player (more if you have the narrow gauge expansion).

Alterations/House Rules: Poker chips; possibly large cubes instead of the paper shares; Erie expansion waits for more experienced play.

Touchstones: Game length variable; Temporary emergent alliainces; Initial auction guides prevailing alliances; Simple options per turn; Fractional incentive management; Tight auctions for very few shares; Asymmetrical; Wabash can break-out of mediocrity if played right; Blocking; Sabotage; 'Gotham gambit' if playing with three.

Broadest structure: Determined by players wanting the short game and those wanting the long game, combined with the particular railroad one buys in the initial auction. The first three auction actions are almost as important.

Sand timers: one-minute minimum, and an invitation to take longer when needed. This is not a game for the impulsive, and breaking the tempo is a good way to remove those 'building' their way to the finish line.

This game responds to temporary alliances.

RATING: 9
A quick-paced stock holding game in the vein of classic train economic games. The game is unique in the way actions are chosen. Select to add trains, make spaces more valuable, or buy shares for a number of times then trigger a payout. It feels like you have more control over the well-being of a company you invested in here. I enjoy how the only money a company has to build trains is the money the players spend to purchase shares. This creates an interesting balance between keeping you money to win vs. investing in the company that may pay you more in the future. The game arc is determined by how much the players choose to spend on shares. Stock games can be a hard sell, but Chicago Express has great quality components and is exciting to play.

RATING: 7
Played Chicago Express version.  Good components, decent enough derivative train game, but the game balance seems a little odd.  Tends to reward people who own the only share in a crappy railroad, since those tend to be worth more than most shares in a very good railroad.  The only really bad decision is to own 1 or 2 of 3 shares in a crap railroad.  This creates a pretty odd incentive for the players, as controlling your railroad well can sometimes be a mistake, as it causes others to put you up for auction, thus ruining your take...  Good stock games have a mechanism to make up for this, like letting the railroad pay its controller at a certain point, either at the end of the game or at some point in between.  Imperial has payouts for the taxation phase.  This game should have the cash remaining in a railroad pay out to the stock owners at the end of the game or something.  OK game.

Second play I liked it better and didn't seem to have the balance issue I complained about at first.  Part of this was experience, I knew when to get off that horse before everyone else tried to get on.  It still doesn't feel thematic to do that, but it doesn't hurt the gameplay and the theme is pretty strong otherwise.  Good game.

RATING: 5.5
I played this once, and found it to be very deep and newbie-unfriendly.  The game only takes an hour, but it requires careful consideration of every move,  because one wrong play (even in the beginning) can make you lose and give the game to someone else.  Each play that you make can radically change the strategies of other players, for good or ill.  That does make it an interesting game if you know how to play and are willing to burn some brain cells in figuring out which action to take, or how much to bid on a particular stock, for example.

This looks like a train game, but it's basically a stock and dividend game, where you can make existing stockholders lose future earnings potential by capitalizing their company to dilute their stock holdings.  Knowing when to capitalize, when to expand track, and when to develop the cities on which existing track lies is the heart of the game.  For example, "If I capitalize this stock, and the player to my left wins it, he'll be in a great place to expand that companies track lines.  Do I want that?  Maybe yes, if I already own that stock -- we can work together to expand it."

Tricky, tricky game, one of the kind that JC likes.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
