RATING: 7
I like Chicago Express reasonably well for what it is. It's a nice compromise between a heavy train game and something like Empire Builder or T2R. However, there are two major caveats. One, you really need to play with people who are of the same skill level for it to be optimal. Inexperienced players who make rash plays tend to unknowingly king make which can be maddeningly frustrating. Two, I find it to be a game that can be really hard to make up ground in. If you get behind, you're pretty much playing for third.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Chicago Express is a nasty, chaotic, but almost wholly transparent beast of an economic railroad game-- the game provides the game state quite readily, but due to its highly overlapping-player-driven structure, the game is chaotic (but not random!). As players collectively invest, divest, sink, or float railroad companies within a variable-length game, the elements of collusion, collision, and conflict are high: alliances shift, meld, and molt as the game progresses. The variable game timer also allows players to disrupt long-term strategies by cutting the game short and punishing short-term strategies by stretching the game out: depends on the current nature of collusive relations!

The entire exercise feels like trying to hold a river: it requires one to let go of the holding and stopping, and learn to divert the flow instead.

Note: This is another game that suffers from "fee air!" syndrome -- some may see this as an opportunity to fit in more train games!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The rules are deceivingly simple, and it is probably the game in my collection where understanding the rules and playing well are two completely different things.

This game has layers of complexity in terms of optimization. The share auction is a very central aspect of the game, and the ability to correctly evaluate the share price is probably going to determine your success in this game.

I still have not played this enough, but I can see this game continue to fascinate me with simple rules and difficult decisions.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Someone had commented that Chicago Express is "18xx Uber-Lite", and they're right. I've played 1870 and several other 18xx games, and was always impressed with the mechanism where players buy stock in a railroad company, but they don't necessarily "own" or run the company. The other fascinating concept was that company money and the players' money were kept separate. The key was to figure out how to make that money that was in the company's coffers to pay out back to the player for the win. However, this was always tainted by the cumbersome upkeep, numerous convoluted rules, and exhausting play time of 4-6 hours. Chicago Express strips it down to the essentials: the stock ownership and separate money ideas are still there, but the track laying, and income generation are much, much more streamlined. There's even an innovative action-tracker mechanism, represented by three dials on the board, to trigger when dividend payouts are made. The more stock one has in the railroad, the more income they make. However, the railroad's major source of income is through issuing stock. If no one buys the stock, it is possible that the railroad will come to a screeching halt in terms of expansion, and hence income for the players. The flavor of small railroad companies emerging amongst the bigger companies in 1870 is preserved by the inclusion of the Wabash Railroad, whose entry is triggered late in the game by someone being first to building from the East Coast to Chicago. Will this fledgling company make an impact on the bottom line? No one knows for sure. The only weakness I see is in re-playability, as the board lends itself to stereotyped opening play. Otherwise, it is perfect when I have an 18xx itch to scratch, but not much time or brainpower/stress to spare. Initial rating '9'.

Update (07/14/09): Played this for the 2nd time. Rules were easier to pick up this time. Wow! 18xx-uber-lite in 60-75 minutes! Got creamed by the guy who had a monopoly on the Philadelphia RR, but did well with the B&O myself. Interesting strategies to figure out. Wabash actually came out much earlier and was pivotal in generating the cash that allowed that player to win. Still concerned about standard "opening" play, as this could get figured-out with a relatively few plays. 

Update (07/04/11) 4 plays: I am starting to get disappointed with this game, as it seems to end too quickly. It scratches the 18xx stock/train itch within a quick hour, but man, it is over just as the fun was beginning. After playing Baltimore & Ohio a number of times, which is thematically and mechanically very similar, I prefer B&O over this any day, due to its more substantial and meatier gameplay. I'm downing this to a '7'.

Update (01/14/12): After playing German Railways, I'd rather play that to get my stock auction fix that CE has, as CE ends too quickly for my tastes. GR has the novel action draw mechanic that makes for exciting gameplay, and is a bit longer for a more satisfying experience. I'm downing this to a '6'.

Update (07/07/12) 6 plays: We brought this out thinking knowing it would be a quick train-stock game that lasts about an hour. We quickly remember why we don't play this anymore, as we're not good at it. The game is so fragile and volatile, one mistake early on could break the game in someone's favor. Within the first turn or two, some of us could all see that the game was getting away from us, and squarely in control of the two richest players, and there was no way to rein them back in. Perhaps we need to play this more to "get it", but for now, there is bad taste in some people's mouths. Groupthink has already concluded that play will be relatively scripted.

RATING: 7
Uhmm... he jugado varias partidas a este juego y me he convencido de que los jugadores deben empezar con la mitad del dinero que establecen las reglas. De lo contrario, la compañía roja adquiere ventaja sobre las demás y, por ende, sus accionistas. Con la mitad de dinero, la compañía roja no tiene asegurado llegar a Chicago por lo que debe obtener ingresos de su trayectoria o esperar a que los accionistas posean más capital. Esto introduce un equilibrio entre todas las compañías: las que ofertan más acciones se harán con más fondos a cambio de menor rentabilidad; las que subastan menos acciones darán mayor beneficio pero a cambio de mayor riesgo y de un desarrollo más lento.
Con la modificación comentada, el juego adquiere auténtico interés y es equilibrado. El jugador puede meterse en la piel de un inversor que busca, unas veces, lograr la mayor rentabilidad y, en otras, debilitar los ingresos de sus rivales.

RATING: 7
I was really looking forward to this as some buds really like this one but it didnt fully "deliver" for me, punn intended of course... :) 

All in all it was good, fast moving, short game time, NO luck, yet something seemed missing for me. Maybe because we only had 3 at the table, maybe it was more subtle that it appeared but I thought overall it lacked depth if anything. 

More play might be needed but so far it just seemed like Acquire where you pick your poisons and run with them. A little more than that but I found some of the decisions uninteresting like developing and building. It really didn't matter a whole lot where you built because developing was virtually the same everywhere you went. There just wasn't much variance which I guess keeps the game tighter but I mean you develop for +1 income or +2, whoopdie do. You build here, or there, developments seem mostly the same everywhere and they are spread out so this makes the building less interesting. Now this is only after one game so maybe I have to eat some of those words we'll see, I'd like to really since I own the game. 

The ONE thing that really mattered though was the screwage, at least in our game. There is a way to get cut right off which I found surprising, so if you don't build fast enough and don't pass the potential "cut-off" that company is going to hell in a handbasket pretty quick. Also, Chicago is a MAJOR bonus, well it isn't hard at all to sabotage someone trying to get there since a company can barely make it as it is with maybe 3 tracks to spare, so anyone that owns only one share to someone's else two or three can just branch off a little and voila, screw job. 

So for me more play is needed to see if this all comes together, due to the auctions and the board I think this would be best with 4.

RATING: 8.5
This is not Age of Steam.  This is not 18xx.  Anyone who tells you that they are similar needs to play more of all three.

Fun, tense, very difficult to calculate, zero luck (except for who starts), and short.  We played our first three games, plus rules explanations and post-session analysis in 3.5 hours.  Most plays are running about 45 minutes.

As we were going over the games afterwards one player asked another, "So do you like this game."  He answered, "Uh, we just spent 45 minutes arguing about optimal opening builds and stock prices.  Yeah, I'm pretty sure this game kicks ass."

This game is filled with agonizing choices, and I love it.

-- Update (13 plays) --
This game is still amazing, but Pampas Railroads has bumped this rating down a bit. I'll play either one at any time, but I have a slight preference for Pampas. One regular in my gaming group prefers Wabash over Pampas, but even he says that it's a razor thin margin.

RATING: 6
Economic engine game that involves buying shares in train companies, and then building the train systems to cities throughout the eastern part of the country.  Interesting in that the money you pay for stock goes to the companies, then the companies themselves pay for the track.  Generally longer and heavier than I usually prefer, but I've enjoyed it all the same when I've played it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
CE edition: Great looking, fast playing. Simple rules, but very deep gameplay. Focus on "playing the players" rather than "playing the system".

While the Queen edition looks like a eurogame, it doesn't really feel like one. Unlike most euros, in CE everything from pacing and timing to money levels in the game is based on the players' actions, rather than on a more fixed framework. The game provides the basic rules skeleton, but the players really, by their actions, determine how the game is going to develop. Think Modern Art with trains. :-)

I like CE a lot.

RATING: 6
A lot has been written about the temporary alliances, and they do seem to form the heart of the game.  Ultimately the deep interaction is what gives the game its replayability.  A lot of game in one hour.  

I'm six plays in now, and seem to be getting worse at the game with each passing play.  Moreover, the game is revealing itself as a sharply negative game at its decision points, by which I mean so many of its decisions are about destroying opportunities rather tahn creating them.  That's perfectly valid but not what I find fun about games.  I respect Chicago Express for its simplicity of rules and depth of gameplay, but I'm not sure I need to play it again.

RATING: 7
Torn in how to rate this right now. So, this is a place holder rating for me until I can get it played again.

It's a pretty good game and I put it on weight with Steel Driver (I may prefer Steel Driver more right now, but I'm not sure). Wabash Cannonball/Chicago Express is pretty easy to learn, it's got lots of tough competition for shares and vicious bidding for the short share companies.

I don't feel the trainy aspects of the game really, but I most definitely feel the company aspect. 

So far, in my 3 games, the Red line has been huge. I have a lot of thoughts on the game, but I'll have to pen them later.

Very competitive, good economic game, but not much train feel for me. I like it a lot, don't love it, and I love being able to burn an action I think you were going to take.

I actually think both versions of this game are great. The Queen set is beautiful, the Winsome set is sparse, but damn portable and completely useful.

I think it's definitely worth playing and you should play it more than once to get a feel for it.

Do not get locked into a style of play and know that you can screw with players by taking null actions just to burn an action track. It's brutal, but very effective.

RATING: 8
A fine light-medium weight railroad game that scales well for two to six players.  Although I haven't yet, I think that it will play best with a full table.

A fairly simple game system, where players are allowed to take only one of three actions on a turn... and, especially later in the game, one action is never enough.  Because there are only three things to do, some games can feel a little repetative.

Because the game virtually forces players to ally through similar share purchases, there can never really be a "I'm playing for myself" feeling. (and, if players allow this to happen, they might as well toss in the towel... giving a player sole control over a company for the entire game is suicide)  Because of this, my rating is lower than it could be.  It's borderline co-operative play with lots of table talk, and I really don't like co-op games.  If you are a major player, help your allies; if not, screw the others at will.

After playing a few more times my rating has gone up, I recommend the game with 2 or 4 players only... when playing with 3, one person invariably gets an extra share at start and can easily halt purchase of at least one more giving him a distinct advantage; with more than 4 at least two players will start without a share which can be devastating in the early rounds.

One last thing... regardless of what the designer's intent (and it's not specified in the rules), play the game with hidden money.  It will add just the right amount of memory element to an already fine game and provide players with that 'extra' element of risk.

RATING: 7
Very tight, well-designed game of great strategic and tactical depth. Involves auction, stocks (including dilution), resource development, and even a bit of board movement tactics (vaguely like Through the Desert or TtR). Winning demands temporary collusion with other players. The parts work together seamlessly: I admire its design.  As a casual gamer and/or a person of limited intellect I'm not sure my brain can calculate all the opportunity costs of the various options each turn. But I find myself still thinking about it, so score could easily go up as I play it more.

RATING: 8
This is a preliminary rating and I have a strong feeling I'll be bumping this up to a nine. It's sort of like a connection-building version of Imperial. There is a lot of subtlety here that will be explored for a while to come - players sharing a vested interest in companies together, and in different company combinations with other players, makes for a lot of options. The game play is wide open strategically and extremely tight mechanically. We had a real tough time understanding how to value the auctions. By the end, looking at those fat company coffers, we realized how grossly most of us had overpaid for things. I'm sure I'll have more to say about this one after more plays.

RATING: 7
A lean, mean, stock manipulation machine. You buy shares in railroad companies. These shares are auctioned, so getting a deal may undercapitalize the company and paying a premium may boost the company's long-term options. Any player with at least one share in a company can take an action on behalf of that company. Actions are simple: auction a share, lay track, or develop industry along the track. 

Alongside Imperial, Chicago Express easily takes the cake for "easy to play, yet somehow totally opaque." The whole game is about predicting and influencing what happens around the table between your turns. You can form temporary alliances with other players but it is a very tricky business. I enjoy this game, but several plays in I don't feel terribly good at it, and don't know what exactly I need to do to improve other than play more. Not a favorite, but fast and reliably interesting.

RATING: 9
Very clean and clever game design. I think at its core, this is a speculation game. Because of the limited number of stock certificates available, the auctions are extremely important moments of the game. And in my book, there's nothing more replayable than an auction system. The mechanic of then playing the board, in response to your opponent's holdings, creates an uncertainty in future value. Brilliant player vs. player interaction.

RATING: 6.5
This is one of the games genres that seem to be over-saturated in the boardgame market. Shares and trains combined.

The game is highly focused on the bidding aspect. Working out the correct value to bid is critical to winning. You can't monopolize a company, because you need other people to help you build, so you'll need shares in various companies that do well.

Its quick, but not my type of game. I've probably seen better implementations elsewhere in other games.

RATING: 7
Economic, trains, transportation - no luck. If we had someone to teach us this game. Auction, network building and stock building. Would be a totally new venue for us. Relatively short playing time but still complex gameplay. It seems this game woould be a very good way to enter into the world of railroad games genre.

Update: We are just scratching the surface of the game, I am still puzzled as to what strategies would be the best to implement. Sure, controlling the game ending is very important, but I am still not sure of whether it is good to own a lot of shares or how many shares you need to collect a good amount of money.
I love how you compete and cooperate at the same time, and this mechanic still fascinates me that you do not own a company but you build up companies together.
I also like how the game mimics certain features of share holding, company development and receiving dividends.
is a puzzling game for many reasons. The auction part is crucial and a very intriguing element in the game, and we are not very experienced in this field yet. Today we had a four player game, and had quite a few aggressive action turns to get the shares we wanted. We will sure have to work on this element, and I am curious as to how we will do next time. The four player game is so much different and tense than the two player one.
It will take time to play CE reasonably well. There are a lot of subtleties in the game.

RATING: 9.5
Fantastic!  I'd much rather play this strategy-dense, streamlined-rules, somewhat-opaque-at-first, 60-to-90-minute game of railroad 'n stock shares than the good (but long and fiddly) railroad game Railroad Tycoon, or the very good (but long and somewhat fiddly) stock shares game Imperial. 
 
'Chicago Express' is notable for handling 6 players very well; you have less control over your fate, but it moves along quickly, turns are short, and you always feel involved trying to reason with your opponents or bidding in stock auctions.

RATING: 10
I'm fascinated by this game: super simple rules and no luck, yet every action has dramatic ramifications. This is an financial beast which can be taught in five minutes and routinely finished in less than an hour. A teaching session will end in an hour and a half or so.

The game's depth makes it a bit unintuitive, and it's easy for newbies to give games away. But I'm perfectly OK with that: I love how the game uses other players as elements of chaos and uncertainty, instead of using dice rolls or card draws for the very same purpose.

Every action matters. Every dollar counts. Newbies will unintentionally give games away, but veterans will too. But that's because the game is deep. Because you don't truly grasp its depth with just a couple plays.

RATING: 10
Other than wargames, the finest thing in gaming is when, as the last action of the game, you auction a stock to dilute existing shares by $2 each, and you beat the holder of one of those shares by $1.

RATING: 8.5
Great game of stock manipulation and train track placement.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Flows smoothly, thanks to quick turns.

Simple, easy-to-grasp rules: I launched the game without explaining the rules to Evan and Jane, and everything went very well.  Details were introduced when needed.

Requires some simple math, and some evaluations of the potential of companies.

I like the simple and effective market model: money goes to the company, which spends it itself in order to expand; revenues are shared among share holders,…

The game situation evolves mostly in a quantitative way: the income of the companies grow and grow.  In my first game, I did not see much blocking (in the mountains/forests) or many share exchanges.  This is very different from games like Agricola, Race for the Galaxy or 7 Wonders (more and more things become doable), or Space Hulk (the tactical situation evolves qualitatively with time).

Despite this, I like Chicago Express, for its streamlined rules, its smooth game flow, and its simple yet interesting market mechanics.

+Chicago Express: Narrow Gauge & Erie Railroad Company
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Even though it may not, strictly speaking, be a eurogame, I think Chicago Express is exhibit A for the case that euro-style design is not yet exhausted, it merely needs to refocus a little. CE is a remarkably straightforward game (with the possible exception of the development tracks for the cities), yet there is a great depth of tactics and enough strategic variety to spawn a myriad of articles on BGG.

Aside from the pure aesthetics of its design, I believe that the use of geometry and the decision to allow players to act in the name of any company they hold stock in, not limiting them to companies they are majority shareholder in, elevate CE above many other euro stock games. The former (largely shared by any train game, admittedly) lets tactical and strategic elements reside in the geometry, which requires little or no explanation, rather than in rules. Game after game show how this is a great way to make a game very deep for its rules weight. The stock-action relationship, meanwhile, avoids the sort of downtime that can be annoying in games like Imperial, as well as the distasteful (albeit realistic) phenomenon of winning the game while doing almost nothing except choosing stocks wisely. In addition, it mitigates the ability of a majority shareholder in a strong company to run away with the game, making (possibly) implicit allegiances a necessity.

The final piece of the puzzle, though, is that Chicago Express packs all of this tension and rich decisions into a 60 minute game. Only Homesteaders, and possibly some of CE’s cousins, manage to match this feat, and it elevates Chicago Express into the top tier of modern strategy games. If it didn’t take so long to set up and tear down, Chicago Express would threaten to make some older strategic euros obsolete.

There are potential problems lurking in the weeds, though, depending on your taste in games. I’ve mentioned the set up and tear down time, the price that must be paid for ample amounts of nice bits. While the tension of having to see if play rolls out in the way you intend can be delicious, it can be frustrating to see players “screw” you by making plays that seem to throw the game to a third party. Likewise, the high degree of player interaction makes kingmaking a possibility. Chicago Express does not give up all of its secrets easily, either, and suffers from related problems. It can be tempting for new players, or as a result of situations that arise when playing with new players, to rush to a conclusion in order to cement a large opening lead. This cuts off the full narrative of the game (especially the founding of the titular Wabash rail company) and may leave a little bit of a bad taste.

Nevertheless, I do not hesitate to call Chicago Express one of the best new games of the last three years, and although there has been a lot of dross released in that time, there have also been a large number of very good and innovative games, so this is no mean feat.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This game packs a lot of depth in a short game length and rather simple ruleset.  On his turn a player can pick one of only three actions, but more than most other games, it is very challenging to evaluate which of the three actions is the best play on any given turn.  For such a streamlined game, at times the play can feel chaotic, but that "chaos" is a result of the difficulty in determining the decisions of other players.  As a player, I often feel like things are spinning out of my control, but that is because I have misplayed the other players.

The genius of this game is that it presents players with many layers of analysis which make seemingly simple decisions - which action of the three do I take this turn - very complex.  The player who can best navigate through all of those layers will position himself in the most profitable railroads at the right times, and win.

Another train game that fails to differentiate itself from the hundreds of other 18xx ripoffs.  Not only is it the same game as most others the quality of the game is atrocious. RATING: 7
I really like train games including the heavy 18XX games.  This is a light to medium train game with a stock component but it does not play like any other train game.  You are invented to gather shares and grow your company but if you are growing quickly, often another player will buy a share and "operate" it so that it does not make more money.  Many traditional 18XX and Steam players don't like this.  

I like the game, it is challenging and fun.  But you do have to know when to sabotage another player's railroad.

RATING: 8
Brilliant game.  No luck, strategic game, highly interactive, cutthroat.  The first time you play it there is so much happening that it doesn't all sink in.  Basically a mathematics/economics game with a spatial component, an emotional element (auctions and fragile alliances), and simple rules.  It feels like a game that has a lot of depth.  Runaway leader problem, and difference of skill/experience problem.  Recommend doing a trial run first, stopping after two dividends, so that everyone gets a better sense of the mechanisms before it's too late -- one false start will cost the match given the no luck and runaway leader problem.  Game can be longer than advertised, easily 2 hours.  Only play with friends who will forgive you for playing competitively, the game does encourage many moves that will destroy another player's chances to win.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
[Played 21 times]
It is an auction game with some nice ideas (e.g. the three kinds of actions triggering the dividends), no luck involved (it feels very dry and mathematic) and a thankfully short playing time (that makes its repetitiveness and no-way-to-recover more acceptable).
The Development action seems seriously weaker than the others (which simplyfies the choices and thus removes some interest from the selection of the actions).
The whole game is in the auctions; the rest seems quite scripted and obvious.
In some configurations (e.g. 2 players) the game just does not work well.

RATING: 7
Interesting tension between the different decisions available.  You have to watch your profit margins carefully.  Much like Modern Art, you don't need to buy a lot if everyone else is paying too much for what they get.  This is a very stripped down game without a lot of frills (in the production or the mechanics), but it challenging.

In my second game (5 players) I won the game after having only bought 2 shares, both in the first round (not the initial auction). 

You have to be very, very careful about how much you spend on shares.  Margins can be very, very tight.

Underagressive bidding can kill you.

2 plays, 3 players
2 play, 4 players
1 play, 5 players

RATING: 7
The ruthlessness of the game belies the "Train" theme, I was ready to dismiss this one as another pointless Train Euro, but as each player get to be real bastards to each other, it's got a lot going for it.

It's like a mini-stockmarket "share purchasing" game really. Companies issue shares to raise capital which in turn is used to build the railway. Getting shares cheap saves you money, but little capital is raised, so the company won't be able to expand, but neither do you want to pay too much (most money wins the game), but also, neither do you want to allow the stock to become too diluted.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Auctions - you quickly realise that the rest of the games actions (of which there are two) are of vastly less importance than the three (or less) auctions that occur each round (of a maximum of eight).

This was the first game of its type (luckless game of incentives, player alliances and stock (or bonds)) that I got to play, and I found it to my liking. Maybe it is more interesting than it is "fun", but damn, it's very interesting and a real challenge.

You have to position yourself carefully - and force the other players to drag you along with them, ensuring that they have to "waste" their action on a non-auction because of the way the capital is distributed. Or you have to come swooping in at an opportune moment to trash that run to Chicago they were so close to completing. It is mean, nasty, full of Zugzwang (to use a chess term) that you can be locked into or use to your advantage, and all in all a great game (though as I said maybe not always a very fun game, the thin veneer of theme doesn't hide the math all that much but that's fine by me)!

My verdict: Slightly difficult to get to the table due to the demands on the players. A great "thinky" game that requires that people invest themselves lest they throw it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Only one game and I can tell it's one I will like. It hits the ground running and doesn't stop until the end. Hopefully others in my game group will be just as excited to play. 

Pros: Interesting game of emerging alliances, beautiful board and bits, relatively short play time, intense, asymmetric and varied games.

Cons: Can get mathy, can have a king-maker element to it, somebody won't like it in the group.

Three plays: 1 4-player, 1 3-player and 1 2-player. 4 and 3 player are definitely better, but the 2-player, in retrospect, was pretty good. It's a different game where it's not as easy to manipulate your opponents. The emerging, temporary alliance aspect of the game is much more difficult to gauge and companies with equal shares among players tend sit around and not move. 

Update after 18 plays (14 of which are 3-player with the same group): A sick, nasty game that is close to my heart. A hard fought victory in CE is better, IMO, than any out there ... it has that little something that a lot of games lack (reminiscent of Dune ... the victories are sweeter and the defeats pure hell).

RATING: 7.5
Fast-moving train game with a simple stock system but a lot of meaningful decisions.  Basically on your turn you can do one of a few things.  Put a stock up for auction (money goes to the company), add track for a company you own at least one stock in, develop land or a city.

Players working together should excel over others.  There is a lot of room for screwage: blocking competing lines, running a line you have a small investment in out to the pasture, etc.  It's quite tactical and requires some good simple math skills to compete.  It wouldn't be extremely exciting except that you can play it within 30-45 minutes and it has a ton of interaction.

Playing with 4 (UPDATE: without the expansion) is tough and someone could get the squeeze.  Playing with 5 or 6 would be ridiculous.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Wow. What a game. Some thoughts, after 1 play:

My group tends to take longer than what's written on the box. From opening this game to teaching it to 4 others to packing everything away: 2 hours. This was unprecedented  in a meaty game.

Everyone liked it a lot. Sucked a new person into the group too, a videogamer who'll now give boardgames a chance!

If we'd had another 90 minutes, would have easily played again.

Game is not as good as Wealth of Nations. However, it's quick to setup and play, with little AP. WoN has a longgg setup and a longggg trade phase. With unlimited time: WoN. When in a crunch (like today), so far this game. Still have a few other economic games to try out, as well as possibly replay Age of Industry, which most of us really liked. But now that this game has been unleashed, the odds of getting AoI to the table have gotten even slimmer :(

Still, this game lives up to the hype. Excellent deep economic gameplay in at most 90 minutes.

Not a true stockholding game. Net worth not counted towards win (just money), buying (no selling) of stocks does not affect a company's value, and no majority shareholder. Taking these into account and thinking of it similar to Power Grid, it's an excellent game.

Initial rating of 8. I actually feel after one play that it's closer to a 9, but we'll see how its longevity holds out, so a more conservative rating.

*Feb 2013* Bumped up to a 9. This game is perfectly balanced and an excellent system. I really like it with 6p!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
My first play of this was tight and intense, and I am not even sure I understand quite yet the vast web of implications behind my actions and choices. Almost intoxicating brain burn as I tried to parse out that web, specifically in how the economic layer of the game, and the meta layer of the game (with the forming and disbanding of alliances) interacts. I thought there would be no negotiation, but in fact there was - and yet, not the kind I am normally used to. Normally negotiation is used to manipulate another player ultimately in a direction of harm, but because of the strange alliance structures that form, much of the negotiation that occurred in our game was cooperative negotiation - pointing out the possible consequences of mutually beneficial choices, or colluding to slow down or stop another player.

I will definitely need to play this a few more times before I really begin to grok it, but I think this may quickly become my favorite game.

-------------------------------------------------

After more plays it's official: This game nears only Diplomacy for me in terms of pleasure of play. It's strategically compelling, the conflict is a totally vicious knife fight, especially in the end game, it's got alliances and a strange strain of negotiation (though I suppose it could just as easily be played in total silence like a game of Pinochle, with players having to predict each others' moves and tactics), it keeps me guessing, it keeps me thinking, it is utterly unforgiving of mistakes, but an intelligent and careful player can even come back from behind. This game is a masterpiece. Simple rules, vastly complex strategy, somehow simultaneously intuitive and counter-intuitive tactics. Almost every single choice a player makes is concurrently helpful and harmful, both to himself and his opponents. This is the ultimate game for me - the game I've been looking for all these years - especially in that it is incredibly tight, plays quickly, and forces me to stop relying on my instincts and make carefully measured choices.

Bumped to 9.9 where I suspect it will stay.

-----------------

Update:

Ok, after several more games, I still really like this, but I am at a point where it's almost frustrating to play rather than enjoyable. There is a skill imbalance in my local playgroup - partly because some of us have played this more than others, and often it feels like if an experienced player ends up allied with one of those less skilled players, there is an insurmountable drag effect. I know that some of the gurus on here would argue that this is also poor play on my part - and perhaps so - with under 20 plays under my belt I am sure I still don't quite grasp some of the more nuanced strategic implications enough to "torque the incentive grid" continually to my advantage.

So what does all this mean? It means the game gets played less and the ratings suffer.

This hits the sweet spot for three people, and it has to be the right 3 (Capizzi and Josh in my case.) Otherwise, it's just not as fun.

RATING: 6.5
Players are rail barons, seeking to invest in promising rail lines and expanding them. The map board shows four starting lines on the east coast: 1) New York Central (green), with 24 available trains and shares, 2) Pennsylvania Railroad Company (red) with 20 trains and 3 shares, 3) Baltimore and Ohio (blue) with 22 trains and 4 shares, 4) Chesapeake and Ohio (yellow) with 26 trains and 6 shares. Players start with $30, and a share in each company is auctioned off. Players then take turns with one of three choices: 1) expand a rail line by up to three trains, 2) develop a rail line to increase income and 3) put a share up for auction. 

When a railroad share is put up for auction, the minimum bid is the income of the railroad divided by the number of owned shares. E.g, at the start of the game, PRC has an income of 7, so the minimum bid on the first share is $7. Money from the auction is put into the railroads money supply, which is used to fund expansions. Expansions cost money per hex, generally $1 per open hex, $4 through mountains and $2 through forests. Expansions into certain hexes, like cities, boost the income of a railroad by one or two. There are some big cities on the map: Wheeling, Pittsburgh and Detriot. A rail line making it into one of these cities has a lucrative boost in income, depending on how developed the city is. For instance, Pittisburgh is worth four extra income, if someone develops Pittsburgh, the income is now worth 6, and a final development gives a total of 8 income. Finally, if rail line makes it into Chicago, the rail line income is boosted by 7, and there is a special dividend pay out.

Dividends occur during the game when two of the three action spaces are full. Each round there can only be a maximum of 5 expansions, 4 developments or 3 auctions. Once two of three possible actions are full, marked by steam gauges, a dividend occurs. Note that a player can choose an action that is available and choose not to execute that action. A railroad pays out the income divided by the number of shares, rounded up. After a dividend, all steam gauges are reset to zero and the development marker for Detriot is moved up by one.

Note when any rail line expands into Chicago, a dividend payout also occurs, but the steam gauges are not reset. After the first rail line makes it into Chicago, a new rail line becomes available: the Wabash line (black), with 2 shares and 11 trains.

The game ends when 1) shares of at least three railroads are all gone, 2) the development marker for Detriot reaches eight, 3) trains from three railroads are all gone, or 4) all development markers are gone.

This rail road stock market game is okay, and has simple mechanics. I dislike the fact where players can maniuplate the ending of the game by buying up shares, or taking an auction action and not do anything to deny someone. But I suppose if you get in the spirit of the game, it will be okay. I'm still trying to learn the nuances of this game, I have a big problem in overpaying for shares. On the plus side, it does play quickly. It is a decent game, but not quite my type of game. Sometimes perception plays a role, and players can deny the leader, putting someone else in the winner's seat. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
A wonderful, quick, and focussed game.  The entire game is played at arms' length, with companies doing the legwork - necessary and yet ultimately irrelevant to your own score.  The trick appears to lie in tempting other players into weakening their own long-term positions, or else nudging them forward while making sure you see some sort of advantage each time.  I'm unreliably woeful at it, but after only a few plays I'm happy to accept I haven't worked it out yet.

RATING: 9
An excellent economic game - meshes together the good feelings I get from playing Container and Imperial: hard economic decisions where the effects of your actions are at first hard to predict, it displays well the investor dilemma (as opposed to owner: your gain is not necessarily a loss for your opponents), you bankroll expansions by infusing capital through share emissions, you build alliances and you switch them when you think it'll maximize your gain. In so far this year (2009), the best game I have played.

I don't think it works as well as some of the others because of the relatively small number of companies (four at first, and a small fifth one later on).  This makes it safe and logical to diversify your holdings as much as you want instead of dealing with the angst of more selective buying and focusing, especially since you only need one share of a company to build track with it.  For a very similar but better game, try American Rails by Tim Harrison.  That's the one to beat, and it deserves a wider audience.

RATING: 4
Having never played 18xx but having read descriptions of the genre, I think this falls into a similar category. My personal preference is not toward path-building, so I have a natural bias against railroad games, and this is very much a member of that. My second complaint with it is that it seems like whoever is helped by 2 opponents will beat whomever is helped by only 1 opponent. Perhaps there is a level of engagement I've overlooked, where an opponent may be helped by one, sabotaged by another, but it does seem like whoever is happy to come in 2nd place will give the 1st place finisher a present. (Similar to the 100K strategy on Survivor that the jury goats employ) This is probably a valid dynamic, but it's not my type of thing. Imperial has a similar thing going, but its rule that only the controlling interest owner may act on the company's (in Imperial's case, country's) behalf makes it much more interesting and thematically consistent than Chicago Express's rule that any shareholder may do the same.

[color=#117bc0][b]Probably rather play:[/b][/color] Imperial

RATING: 7
規則:
*三個指針移到綠色位置，每個顏色火車頭跟股票放到對應個別圖板或主圖板，，每個公司的圓片放在對應的收入軌(黑色不放)，房子放到對應供應區，工業城市軌三個軌分別放一個房子在有數字的第一格，四種顏色起始格放對應火車
*總共發120圓，平均給每個人，之後照紅藍黃綠分別競標一張股票，起始價格等於對應收入，最年長的起標，跳過出價的蓋輪就不能再出，花的錢給公司，沒人出則起標的免費拿，拿到的成為下張股票的起標者，紅色股票持有者為起始玩家
*遊戲照順位輪流選擇三個行動並轉對應指針，可選擇不動作，到紅色的不能選，兩個到紅色則進入分紅階段然後重設回綠色
>競標股票 選一張股票(黑色要火車進場才能選)競標，並且出該公司收入軌除掉賣出股票張數(包括競標的這張)的底價或跳過，無條件向上取整，最高價者出錢給公司拿走該股票，所有人跳過股票放回
>擴展鐵路 至少要有一張該公司股票，放最多三個火車，要跟起始相連，公司出錢，金額未放置格的紅色數字乘上該格的火車數(包括你新放的)，然後該公司收入軌根據該格黑色數字增加(有房子的則額外加房子的)，工業軌則影響特定的城市格
*樹林跟山脈只能有一個火車放置
*當有任何一家公司放火車到芝加哥則進入芝加哥階段
>發展 有房子的格且有火車可放置多一個房子上去，或是提升工業軌一格(也至少要有一個火車在上面)
*底特律不能發展，每次分紅階段自動提升一格，不管有沒有火車
*放在樹林時，上面的火車公司拿兩圓，不影響收入軌
**分紅階段 每個玩家根據公司收入軌除股票張數(無條件向上取整)，每張股票拿對應金錢，行動轉輪回綠色，底特律發展軌右移
**芝加哥階段 進入芝加哥的公司調整收入軌，然後馬上進行一次分紅，第一次進入黑色火車供四會進場，放火車到起始城市，黑色標記放1，並競標第一張黑色股票，由觸發的玩家起標
*遊戲在3家公司沒火車或沒股票或供應區少於等於3間房子或是底特律軌到8時遊戲結束，馬上再進入一次分紅階段，之後錢多獲勝
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Wonderful game. Simple, deep, fast, intense. Wow.

Chicago Express is a share holding train themed game. It's 90% auction, 10% route building - the game is won or lost in the auction phase. The goal is to have the most money [i]when the game ends[/i]. 

Problem is, game length depends heavily on the rate at which company shares are sold, and this is under complete control of the (collective) players. Each player has a 'posture' that favours either long game or a short game (based on current cash and rate of change of cash) - with more rounds come more dividends, so a low cash, high income player will have time to overtake a high cash, low income player, and vice versa.

Another key aspect is manipulating other players incentives. Each player can only help one company at a time, so if you can get the shares to fall out of the auction just right, the other players will be forced to help you as a side effect of helping themselves.

Nice components, zero luck, the depth of a 2hr + game packed into 1 hr. There's plenty of interaction, sabotage and blocking.

This is my favourite game since late 2009.
Bought Jul 2009

RATING: 6
The thing that bugs me about this game is I can't work out the good plays. It seems that who wins the next auction tends to have an overwhelming effect on how the next part of the game develops (if it's a, the game goes this way, if it's b a completely different way) and it feels as if you're mostly just taken along for the ride. Put another way, a share that you buy is worth more if people don't buy any more of it, and less if more of it is put up for sale. But you don't know what people will do, so any valuation you make is a complete guess as to intentions. And that's not a process or a result that I enjoyed.

RATING: 10
Combines the stock holding and expansionist drive of Imperial with the route planning and blocking of Through the Desert. It plays quickly. The pace is exciting as the railroads race toward Chicago. 

Often, players can't decide whether to collude, sabotage each other, or a little of both.  If the Wabash opens up, it throws a wrench into the endgame gearbox. No two games are much alike.  As players become experienced with its subtleties, play becomes more nasty.

Wabash Cannonball is supposed to be an open information game, but my girlfriend and some of my gamer friends strongly prefer hidden money.  I often play with hidden money to accommodate them and I don't think it breaks the game.  It is very good with three or four players.  The addition of the Erie makes it just as good with five.

I have a home made copy with my own made-from-scratch (photoshop) board:
http://www.boardgamegeek.com/image/355477

I was thinking I'd buy a Queen edition, but was disappointed that they re-named it Chicago Express.  Lame!

RATING: 5
I really don't have any knowledge of how the financial world works, but I like how the share system is implemented in this game: money from the shares auctioned goes into a fund which helps finance the building of the same coloured rail network. Building the network into cities and adding 'city developments' increases the value of the shares, which at various times in the game reward the shareholders some more income. I like the way this interesting environment has been implemented very smoothly and simply. Yet really the networks all start in one corner with the focus of heading for Chicago in the opposite corner, so no physical variety. The only changes are mentally mathematical whereas I rate high replayability on refreshing physical setups (Settlers, Africa) or changes in hand contents.

RATING: 8
Here players invest in company's stocks, and then make decisions as to where a company you have invested in puts down train pieces. Each hex has a modifier and a cost, which comes out of the company money, and then the income level of that company rises. On a player's turn they can either put a stock up for bid, which they won't necessarily win for, or they can lay down trains, or they can build houses on the board improving the value of rail lines touching the house. The number of times per phase that each action can be taken is measured with a dial, and once two dials have hit the red, the companies pay out dividends to the stock holders, and then all actions are available again. The game ends when one of several conditions occur. It actually does take about an hour.

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:star: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:star: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:star: Multiple plays stay interesting
:star: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::star::nostar::nostar: Raw enjoyment

RATING: N/A
One I bring to every game night and would play once a week if it were ever possible. 

Share dilution is a wonky thing to try and wrap your brain around, but it means that the game is not simply another in the long (very long) line of "auction games that are about True Price Discovery and straight ROI." Rules-wise, the game is quite simple: auction and add the winning bid to the company treasury, build track and pay for it out of a company treasury, develop a hex, or claim an action and do not take it, thus denying it to others. But this game dispenses entirely with downtime. Because you know what happens when it's not your turn? Your allies change, and you need to make sure you always know who your friends are, and exactly when they are no longer your friend, and why. 

That it all plays out in around an hour is awesome. Eleven plays in and I feel like I've just started to scratch the surface of this one.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This game is a brilliant. It's to train games what Memoir '44 is to war games.

Players take turns putting train company stocks up for auction, building train tracks and developing the spaces on the board.  As the game progress the value of each train company increases.  The value of a stock is the value of the train company divided by the number of its stock currently in play.

Because of the shared ownership of the train companies every action a player takes will help and hurt all the other players.  Players have to exploit this web of interdependence in order to make more money than their opponents.  That such complex interaction can arise from so simple a rule set makes for an amazingly fun game.

RATING: 10
It's fast (30 minutes), full of complexities, has an amazing player range (3-6), is easy to learn and has an elegant art style and game design.

But all this masks... TREACHERY and SABOTAGE! Alliances are made, then SHATTERED.

After playing it more and more, I think I still really appreciate this game. Again, what needs to be emphasized is that it plays fast, it has relatively simple rules and the gameplay is really a mind-game at heart as it really is about manipulating opponents over any individual good play. This is due to the game state being extremely sensitive as one bad play by any player can has disastrous effects for the rest of the game.

I really respect this game and feel that not many games are like it. HOWEVER, it's a huge newbie trap. If you have 3 good gamers and 1 novice, that novice is going to determine the game. He might make one silly move such as allowing a share to go cheap to one player and that will often determine the game results right then and there.

The other aspects to look at is that the game usually ends on rounds 3 or 4. People usually just auction or build, so theoretically in a 4 round, 4 player game, you might only have 8 actions to do in a whole. If you mess up on one of your turns, you've screwed about 12.5% of your game, a huge amount.

So with that said, with the right players, this game CAN be a very good one. It can be very tense, neck-to-neck and more interesting than your stone-cold Euro. However, most of the time, at least for myself, I find the game to flop hard because I usually play with gamers that are not used to Chicago Express. They do silly things such as help a company as a minority and give someone else the win without seeing in the first place. The game can also be pretty mathy sometimes.

So the potential for being an excellent game is there, but most of the time, the game bombs because of the nature of the people you run into. Not recommended for very casual groups.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I wasn't impressed with Chicago Express as I thought I would be.  For one thing, I expected it to be more like Imperial than it was.  It's quite similar to Imperial in how it plays, but it's got a much shorter play time, so it's much harder to manipulate things so that one or two players don't have a clear advantage.  This gives Chicago Express a totally different feel, and one that I don't enjoy very much.  I can see that Chicago Express is a great game.  But I think it's too brutal for me to have very much fun playing it.

I would certainly play it again though because I didn't like Imperial very much on my first play and now it's one of my favorites.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
A shorter version of Prarie or Pampas Railroads. Unfortunately I think it loses some of the key 'logic' of its ancestors, but is certainly made a bit more elegant by dumping the crayons. There are several issues: the biggest is that shares at the game start at undervalued - they sell for perhaps 20% of their worth. They can't sell for more because no-one has enough money, and if you pay too much, the other players can then buy more shares even cheaper. By game end the value is much lower and calculable, and shares should go for very close to 100% of their value. In the mid-game, share prices are highest (though the value is lower than at start) and the limited stock offerings only let them gradually approach the true value. This makes the game very sensitive to the starting conditions: spending one dollar too much may let someone else buy an extra share handing them the game. If you are left as the sole single share-holder in a company for a turn or two you'll make out like a bandit, but it is not something that you can force to happen. Minor blemishes are that the 'develop' action is under-powered except for use as a timing mechanism, and the building costs in mountains and forests could easily have been made fixed rather than randomly varying by a dollar.

RATING: 9.5
Wabash Cannonball / Chicago Express is a quick-playing, auction game of railroad expansion and shareholding. 

There is no luck and the game is about acting/reacting to what others are doing. Through clever manipulation of available actions and auctioning of shares, you can influence other players into working together to a common end, if only temporarily. While a major feature of the game is the auction, there are also many other things you can do to mess with other players' plans (try taking the B&O or even the PRR southwest to block the C&O - when done properly, it's a thing of beauty!). 

The game comes down to influencing when the game will end (if I can end it next turn, I will win, but one turn too early or too late and I don't have a chance). Sometimes the game will end after 4 dividends, sometimes more, and rarely the full 8. Sometimes railroads will make it to Chicago and Detroit; often no one does. If you believe the game always plays out the same and get stale, you don't understand the game and haven't played it enough.

The rules are simple and the depth is subtle at first. Give it a few plays - this is an amazing game!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Two immediate thoughts after playing for the first time:
1) What just happened?
2) May I have some more?

The dynamic of buying and selling shares is so, so interesting. Purchasing more shares of a company which you are already vested in is all well and good, but if someone buys into shares of a company you, too, own, it reduces your dividends. The truth is, this game is so fluid and player-driven that such situations are merely inevitable. Only the timing is unpredictable. As the game progresses, people have more money but less opportunities to buy more shares, and so it becomes a game of valuation and judging the worth of allying with others or tanking a company and jumping ship (train?). And so, it becomes a game of playing the players. It becomes a game JUST for me. I am a little abashed that I waited so long to play this game, as it has skyrockted to be an easy favorite. All this offered in the span of an hour or so, even at maximum players? Splendid!

(+) Very straightforward rules yet rife with subtleties in practice. Opaque as all get-out, and that's a good thing in my book.
(+) Heavy player interaction, formation and breaking of alliances, and shared incentives.
(+) Great component quality (not a primary selling point for me).

(-) Vicious gameplay - not for wimps. There are many opportunities to cut the knees of your once allied player. This is not a game for those who do not like their plans being spoiled.
(-) A 'gamers' game - thus not accessible, despite its quick gameplay. You need strong game sense to correctly assess the situation.
(-) The game's theme itself comes across as dry and "math-y", which can be off-putting to certain demographics, but muscling players around is the lifeblood of the game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Updated 3/18/16

I cannot stop thinking about this game. It's invading my consciousness.


---

This is like Modern Art, Hardcore Thrash Death Metal edition. The complexity-to-rule-load ratio is really, really impressive. And the possibility space for interaction is pleasingly huge. It's a Lot of Stock Game in a pretty short space of time.

It's a bit clunky, and there are chunks of time where nothing's going on for you - it doesn't have, say, the Knizia ultra-smoothness and perpetual engagement - but the *depth* kind of hits me upside the head with pleasure...

RATING: 8.5
--Jul2008-- Hadn't intended to buy a copy, but a first play was quite compelling.  The game is short and delightfully subtle; I'll be very happy if I could play it regularly.

Over in the _Paris Connection_ page, I wrote "Wabash/CE is about making more money than the opponents. That's it. One can get there by constructive actions (making one's investments pay out more than those of one's colleagues) or by deconstructive actions (making one's opponents investments pay out less than one's own.) Either way works; either way is viable; either way is the way the game is 'supposed' to go."  I've not done better since.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Decent light game with some easy decisions to be made. Calculations are basic and shouldn't slow down the play near the end of the game. Games should be close between players. Everything ties in quite nicely, but not sure how much replay ability I'll find in this.

I find that (a bit like in Modern Art), a player who plays in a strange way can really mess up logical moves and suck the enjoyment right out of this. You can be left in a position where you're just kingmaking as a passenger along for the ride. Be someone's bitch and help them win, or try and help no one by making sub optimal moves while trying to extract yourself away from a fellow shareholder. I don't like to be in this position, or to see another player in this position. It seems to be the nature of the mechanic where share owners can build as they please or put shares up for auction to directly mess with a specific player. It's actually very aggressive - far more than some games with direct military confrontation, even if it's masked fairly innocuously as a simple train building and share buying game.

Some games are quite good, while others are very bad. This inconsistency leads me to my rating. On any given playing my rating could go up or down a notch.

One final thought: I'm left wondering whether the game would play better if each player received $1,000 starting money. The low starting capital seems to me to spoil the game somewhat. Early in the game you're buying the shares cheap because there's just not much money there. Near the end the game degenerates into a lame mathematical exercise of share worth that can be reasonably accurately calculated. Basic arithmetic cannot really be considered as fun.

RATING: 6
Like a good story a good game generally has a narrative arc with tension slowly building towards an end-game climax/resolution. Chicago Express does this backwards. For me it felt like the game mostly got played over the first two to four turns when the decision space was still large enough to be interesting, but after that the outcome seemed pretty much predetermined and the whole thing played out on autopilot (for what felt like an agonizingly long time). 

The game is especially punishing for first-time players, since there is zero context for accurately valuing the early, and absolutely critical, auctions.

RATING: 7
The action dials are cute (I've noticed I have a weak spot for games with dials), and I love how they track the variable length of each round of play. Unlike other collusion/investment games, it is not [i]if[/i] (Modern Art, Neue Heimat), or [i]when[/i] (Imperial), but [i]how many times[/i] your investment will pay out. Your opponents' bids often reflect how many times they think it will pay out, and give you clues on what they plan to do with future actions.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Purchased Wabash Cannonball version on 01/04/08. Thanks JC!

No-luck shares game. Players bid for shares to different railroad companies to start the game. During the game, each turn consists of an action choice: expand a railroad, develop a hex or auction a share.

When two of three choices are maxed out, a dividend  on all companies is paid out. Rinse and repeat.

In order to do well at this game, you need to be able to crunch numbers and bid correctly for shares. Overpay and you will lose. The trick is to control the value of a railroad with someone else BUT have just that one share more than them.

Chicago express has nice bits but a dividends table would be useful.

RATING: 7.5
This is a charming little stock game that benefits from being short.  I love the track building and appreciate the way the turns play out.  What I'm not a fan of though is the results of the extreme nature of the sparse stocks.  While it certainly isn't going to happen in every game, players can get closed out of worthwhile stock and have nothing to do put become a kingmaker until the end of the game as there is no way to actually improve your position.  With more players the higher the likelihood of this happening.  This is of course why the length works to its benefit.  If it were longer it would be unbearable.  

After a number of plays this is best (for my taste) with three.

UPDATE: If you play with four, force the game into a speed version.  It makes for more entertaining results as often a winner is already determined halfway through the game if you have the time to figure it out.

Perhaps Congo Line does the minimal stock game better...

I'm looking forward to to the expansion as it so perfectly fixes my issues with the 4 player game.  If a player is getting closed out they take Erie and play their own game.

All in all it felt like a heavily watered down version of 18xx with all the excitement removed.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Chicago Express is a no-luck incentive manipulation game with difficult-to-predict butterfly effects. It's all about free riding on the efforts of others, setting up incentives to your advantage, and riding through the groupthink. But that's hard to do because the effects of actions are neither obvious nor immediate. There are strategic considerations but it is not easy to see them.  Victory (or defeat) results not from the singular but rather the composite results of every player's actions. Yet a loss can often be traced back, retrospectively, to a few key decisions.

All of this means that the game benefits from an experienced group. It's in the same genre as Imperial. What I like about Imperial I like about Chicago Express. Lower overhead and shorter playing time mean that I think I prefer Chicago Express. It's easily among the deepest, most strategic games you can play with a group in under an hour. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Chicago Express/Wabash Cannonball was my gateway to the world of train games that don't begin with the word, "Ticket." It usually delivers a tight, tense game with experienced players, but it's relatively easy to teach new players too. Not hard to pick up, but a challenge to win. Zero randomness in this game, other than player seating order.

RATING: 6.5
Cool game with horrible board! The game plays very quickly but with lots of decisions: which action to choose, which rail line to expand, how much to spend on auctions. For true fans of 18xx, this is NOT an 18xx game. But for me, it feels kind of like one to me: trains, companies, shares, your money vs. company money. There is, however, no moving of goods. Also, game play is very streamlined: the game plays quickly.

Good game! It depends heavily on correctly valuing the shares in the auctions, I think -- I wonder if it will hold up to repeated play (as people in a group come to some sort of consensus as to the value of the different companies)?

--- Summary ---
There are 5 companies, plus Wabash Cannonball. Each has a starting value shown on the board. One share of each company is auctioned off, then the game begins. In all auctions, the minimum bid equals the dividend value if dividends were to be issued after the share up for bid were sold, which is (company value) / (total outstanding shares), rounded up to the next integer. 

On your turn, you select and resolve an action. There are 3 types, which may be chosen x, y and z times per round (marker on board records choices as they are taken); the round ends when any 2 of these action types have been chosen the maximum number of times. The actions are: 
- capitalize (put a share up for auction, if one is available; $ comes from your personal money)
- build track (all start on east coast in start cities; pay applicable terrain cost; limits on # companies with track in certain hexes; $ comes from company money; as you build into cities and other hexes, company income improves -- move marker immediately)
- develop (put development marker on some hexes [e.g., build mines, develop one of the 3 industrial cities, etc.]; increases value of hex to companies with track there -- their income marker moves immediately). When a company connects to Chicago, dividends are paid immediately; also, Wabash Cannonball becomes available. 

When the round ends, all companies pay dividends. Reset action markers. Detroit's value increases by 1. The game ends if Detroit is at value 8, 3  companies have built all their track, 3  companies have sold all shares, or if all cash in game is used (I think). If not ended, repeat above.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
The board is prettier than Winsome's version, but I find it a bit on the busy side! But it's almost exactly the same game.

The game plays very quickly but with lots of decisions: which action to choose, which rail line to expand, how much to spend on auctions. For true fans of 18xx, this is NOT an 18xx game. But for me, it feels kind of like one to me: trains, companies, shares, your money vs. company money. There is, however, no moving of goods. Also, game play is very streamlined: the game plays quickly.

Good game! It depends heavily on correctly valuing the shares in the auctions, so (as most auction games) it might be tough playing in groups with mixed experience levels. 

--- Summary ---
There are 5 companies, plus Wabash Cannonball. Each has a starting value shown on the board. One share of each company is auctioned off, then the game begins. In all auctions, the minimum bid equals the dividend value if dividends were to be issued after the share up for bid were sold, which is (company value) / (total outstanding shares), rounded up to the next integer. 

On your turn, you select and resolve an action. There are 3 types, which may be chosen x, y and z times per round (marker on board records choices as they are taken); the round ends when any 2 of these action types have been chosen the maximum number of times. The actions are: 
- capitalize (put a share up for auction, if one is available; $ comes from your personal money)
- build track (all start on east coast in start cities; pay applicable terrain cost; limits on # companies with track in certain hexes; $ comes from company money; as you build into cities and other hexes, company income improves -- move marker immediately)
- develop (put development marker on some hexes [e.g., build mines, develop one of the 3 industrial cities, etc.]; increases value of hex to companies with track there -- their income marker moves immediately). When a company connects to Chicago, dividends are paid immediately; also, Wabash Cannonball becomes available. 

When the round ends, all companies pay dividends. Reset action markers. Detroit's value increases by 1. The game ends if Detroit is at value 8, 3  companies have built all their track, 3  companies have sold all shares, or if all cash in game is used (I think). If not ended, repeat above.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Chicago Express is an easy to pick up train game with stocks.   You start with a stock auction and each of four companies auction off one stock.  Then players take turns in clockwise order executing actions that range from building track(to extend the rail line) to developing (increasing the stock value of the railroad company), to auctioning off more stock.  

The game seemed rather tenuous.  You have to be on constant watch for how much money you have vs. how much money others have.  This is deathly important in order to stay on top of possible auctions that would dilute your stock or cause you to lose the majority.  I liked the tension but overall the game seemed rather light and short.

RATING: 9.5
I kept the original Winsome game and sold the (I feel) overproduced Queen Games version.  Don't need the fanciness that takes up so much more shelf space!

Extremely tight economic game which plays fantastically amongst ultra-competitive gamers who understand the "player outside the game" mechanic--in other words the player is not part of the game, but rather outside it and manipulating everything without undue ties to any particular company.

Really the only thing keeping it from a 10 rating is the length.  I tend to favor 10s for games that I love but which neither overstay, nor, as in this case, understay their welcome.  An odd complaint perhaps but I'd like to see a slightly slower build to crescendo across a more sweeping playtime.  Which is why I'm looking forward to this years Essen package, as this system is being transplanted into more of a "gamers' game!"

RATING: 7
Light economic train game based on stocks and companies.

+ Light - Even a family can play. Straightforward rules, simple actions.
+ Fast - Plays in less than an hour.
+ Interesting - Although simple it is nicely designed to make timing and decisions matter. Speculation of different aspects (cost/effectiveness of a share, time remaining before game end).
+ Interactive - Shifting alliances, action "burning", hate-bidding, path blocking etc. create a very interactive environment.

- Not good with 3 - Needs 4-5 players to show its potential.

RATING: 10
My initial rating is an 8. I really enjoy the length to depth ratio of this game. The number of vectors you can use to manipulate both players and stock values is surprisingly high. I want to play more.

UPDATE: I just raised my rating to a 9. This will make my revised top ten list when I have the time to do a full re-assessment. In short, every game is a new experience where I learn something about both long and short term strategy. The short play time is packed with decisions and means that the game never overstays its welcome. Lovely.

Update II: I played last night with six players. Wow. That was different. You have less turns per round, which means less control. Add the fact that three players were brand new Chicago Express players, and the game was all over the place. I was dead last because my typical strategies simply didn't work. RATING: 7.5
Opinion after 1st play of the 3 player game.

On 1st glance it feels like it'd be a very dry game, but coming away from our 1st game we felt quite the opposite.
The pace of the game, fluidity of the auctions, and strategy of action choices, helped keep it interest throughout.

The complete absence of any luck is very interesting; it's solely the actions, and reactions, of players that determines their strategies.
So not unlike Chess, it's a puzzle game of near-impossible complexity; similarly it can be appreciated at the novice level just as easily as at higher levels.

Component quality seems good, though I wish the money was larger.
Bought for £21 on Amazon.co.uk; an absolute bargain I think.

RATING: 8
Chicago Express (originally known as Wabash Cannonball when it was released by Winsome Games in 2007) is a game that I’ve been wanting to try for a while. It’s commonly referred to as providing the flavor of an 18xx train game in a short package. Games that are actually part of the 18xx series can take many, many hours to play, but I’ve been intrigued by those train games for some time now. Having the chance to get the flavor of one of those epic games without devoting the long time to learning the rules and playing the game sounded perfect to me. Thus, my expectations for Chicago Express were fairly high, especially since I’d been wanting to play it for over a year. Unlike many game, this one actually lived up perfectly to my expectations. It was short and sweet (roughly 60 minutes even for first-timers) and packed quite a punch! For such a quick game, it was remarkably dense, packing in more decisions per minute than just about any other game I’ve played. I wish the game was packed even half as densely into its game box since the box for Chicago Express is at least twice as big as it needs to be, and fitting it on the shelves of my tiny New York City apartment is going to be a challenge, but it’s a testament to how enjoyable the game was that I’m willing to try to make it fit. Simply deciding on each turn whether to develop a hex, expand a rail line, or auction a share in a train company, is a wonderfully tense decision, let alone deciding which hex to develop, or where to expand the rail line (and which rail line to expand), or how much to bid for a share (and which train company’s share to put up for auction). It’s definitely one of those clichéd rule sets that is easy to learn and hard to master.

Received as gift: Christmas 2008
