RATING: 6
Chicago Express is an older game. It's a train/route/stock market game. That already tells you most of what the game is about. There are a few twists that make this different from other games of the type. First there's three types of actions (start and auction, build track, and upgrade a hex) you can take on your turn and each action can only be taken so many times in a round (represented by moving the dial up on that gauge). When two gauges max out, a dividend round is triggered. One of the profitable moves in the game is getting a train company to Chicago. There's also an extra company that is brought into the game when someone reaches Chicago and that company doesn't have to start on the east coast, and thus can reach Chicago fairly easily.

These differences are interesting, but it doesn't really stand out much from the genre. I'd be as happy to play this as I would most games of this ilk.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
About [thing=31730][/thing]:

[b]1) What is it?[/b]
[thing=31730][/thing] is an economic and strategic game with auctions and route building. But this really doens't say what [thing=31730][/thing] is. Consider this: 18xx was feeling a bit down - everything in its life was taking too long, and while he had some good and faithful friends, there are some places it simply couldn't go - and it wanted to connect to everything and everyone. Therefore, it, understandably, was devaluating itself in the local pub. 18xx met another game there that night and, in a vulnerable and needy mood, they had a torrid night of love.

Nine months later [thing=31730][/thing] was born. The game 18xx met that night? [thing=9209][/thing].

[thing=31730][/thing] took all the strategic value, the no luck factor in gameplay, and shares of companies and auctions, of the 18xx series. And got the broad appeal, the easiness to play and teach, with a fast playing time, of [thing=9209][/thing]. [thing=31730][/thing] is almost the perfect middle ground between these two games.

[thing=31730][/thing] is rich in options and allows for constant interaction between players, not only with the action themselves, but also by simply picking one, as they are limited in a given round. Timming and a good route are key to make a company prosper - good to attract more investment to keep growing, but also brings the interest of other tycoons.

Overall, [thing=31730][/thing] is a really simple game, with a very good depth coming of such a simple set of rules. I was taken aback seeing how clean the design is, how clear the options are, how few things can be done and so few in a round, and yet, all comes together nicely to produce a great experience, with several ways to proceed to victory.

[b]2) How do you play?[/b]
A play lasts a maximum of 8 rounds. In each round, the players take turns choosing one of three available actions:
- auctioning a share of one of the railroad companies (paying with personal money);
- expanding the rail system of one of the railroad companies (paying with the company's money);
- develop one of the boards hexes.

Of each of those actions only a limited number is available in each round. Expanding the rails and developing rise the value of the companies.

When two of the available actions reach their limits, a dividend phase is executed, where all players gain income from the railroad companies they have shares of. After this dividend phase, the number of actions is reset and a new round begins.

The game ends after 8 rounds or if one of the following conditions is met:
- 3 or more companies have no more locomotives;
- 3 or more companies have no more shares;
- The general supply contains only 3 houses or fewer.

The player with the most money will be the winner.

[b]3) Which are the decisions made during play?[/b]
The main is: which of the three action to do? Picking from 3 isn't all that hard, right? Except that they are limited, and you might not be able to do even do more than two actions in a round. There is a constant need to prioritize.

All three actions have branching decisions. Start an auction? Of which share? Shares are limited and different companies have different amounts of them. The more they have, bigger will be the cake to share (they also have more trains to put on the board), but also the more eaters. Auctioning one share can be made to cut down the dividends of a company, as the total value of a company is divided by the numbers of shares, to see how much each will pay. Finally, how much to pay in a auction is also present, considering the possibilites of future dividends (for you or someone else) and also the amount of money the company needs/have to continue building their route.

Expand a route can only be done to companies the player have shares. And the place to put the trains, of course, matters a lot. They are the main way to rise the value of companies. It is hard (but possible) to block other companies from reaching certain spots. This isn't a blocking game. The goal is do the best possible, going to the best places first - as putting trains become increasingly costier as more companies already are present in a given spot. This tends to be the action used more often and can make or break your game.

Developing is the easier action to do. It direct as the other: simply advance the track of one of the main cities, or put a house on the board - both of these rise the value of a company passing by that place. It is an easier decision because there is usually no doubt the places you receive the most and given nothing to others.

Of course, the shares division between players and the state of the board (routes and developments) give different importance and timming issues for all these decisions during play.

[b]4) What are the good things in the game?[/b]
- Very smart desing: clean, easy and to the point, while also providing depth and paths to victory;
- Easy to teach and to play;
- No luck;
- The art and graphic design are well done;
- Short playing time and providing constant and important decisions throughout;
- Language independant;
- Offers a good amount of player interaction, but isn't too much cutthroat;
- Can accommodate up to 6 players - best played with 4, but works well with 3 and 5 also.

[b]5) Which are the bad news?[/b]
- Though the learning curve isn't steep, the absence of luck in the game do makes experienced players tougher to beat, and this can turn some new players down;
- It can be seen as too simplistic and be overlooked;
- Ends fast, maybe too fast to the taste of some players, that prefer a longer game to develop a strategy.

[b]6) How do you feel while playing?[/b]
Like tycooning is the easiest job around, the problem is the others that want a slice of your sweet pie - there is no problem in you wanting a piece of theirs, it is just fair. You just need to find the best way to make the money flowing in your way and cut others dry. But their money could be useful to keep a company afloat, still able to compete with the others, stretching to higher costs places and more valuable ones. There is a fine balance in all of this. [thing=31730][/thing] is like a walk through a maze: walking, running and jumping are pretty straightforward actions that you can do easily on their own. But, in spite the maze only requiring these three things for you to reach the center, getting there can be a big challenge. Specially with minotycoons going after you.

[thing=31730][/thing] was a big surprise, being so quick, clean and easy, but with a nice depth and lots of points of interaction. These is what designs once were and are harder to find nowadays, with so many mix-and-match in order to create something new, exciting, that calls for attention, even if only for a brief moment.

In the end, [thing=31730][/thing] is a very good step up from [thing=9209][/thing], entering into the path of shares, routes and economy in the train world. Very recommended.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I found this less opaque than German Railways (and probably Texas & Pacific also). While I thought having all the railroads start on the east side needing to head west was a dull idea, it does provide direction on what to do and how "well" each railroad is doing.

RATING: 8
Good game that is suspenseful from the start, since the different end game conditions can make for a significant difference in the number of rounds that are played each game. RATING: 7
First impression: 
Given the short playing time (only 90 minutes!) and simple actions available each turn (only 3!), Chicago Express still comes out as a highly interactive and challenging design that is optimum for 4 players. The theme is quite strong as players build a network of tracks from the Eastern sea board to Chicago. The game is one of few where players delicately balance cooperative game play while pushing individual interests as there is only one victor. To win, one must get other players to align their interests with your own and hope they can propel you to victory. Not many games I can think incorporate these features well.

The design for Chicago Express is well thought out and also exquisite in the sense that the spatial elements in the map feels balanced and  well tested. The forests and mountain range in the middle slows down track progression, forces players to compete for resources and also potentially blocks progression of specific tracks. The cities are well spread out with important industrial cities scattered in the right areas. For example, the rush to Detroit while not easy and away in one corner, is well worth the effort. The asymmetric map is clearly well-developed. The appearance of Wabash late in the game seems low impact seeing that it is not clear how could generate wealth in such a short period of time. Still, no one decided to jump on board the black line. 

If there is one area that deserves some criticism is that Queen Games, like all their other games, decide to package CE in such a horrendously large box. Given how limited space can get,  this is particularly galling as most of the game components can fit in a box half the size. The components while serviceable, are nothing to toot about. Paper money is ok even though most folks would balk at it. Because the game requires constant checks to find out how much money companies own, this game certainly would have benefited from poker chips.. I would imagine the wear and tear to be quite rapid.

Overall, the design is superb. The game play is stellar and the production values are below average.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Fan-freakin'-tastic! Just the right mix of easy to learn, difficult to master, my prime example of just how good Winsome games can be. If I have one complaint, it's that my group's love of this game has made it harder to try other Winsome designs that I'm keen on exploring, but when the go-to game is this good, I'm not super sad about it!

If there's one complaint, it's that the game still remains largely opaque even this many plays in. While this is a feature rather than a bug (most of the game is figuring out how to play against the other players), it does mean that it takes a play or two for new players to wrap their heads around it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Stock speculation on train companies. As with Acquire, nobody owns the companies per se, they only control them through the shares they purchase. The game has a fairly simple set of rules - you basically have 3 options plus the possibility to null your action each turn and all of the information is open and there is no luck mechanism. Nevertheless, there is a rich array of tactical possibilities each turn - it's one of those games where repeated play will reveal the potential strategies. The stock dilution aspect creates temporary and ever-changing alliances resulting in players sharing the task of growing their favorite companies at the expense of others. The wooden components and game art are beautifully produced. The little dials to record the actions never fail to impress new players. Plays well at 3-4 players although I did attempt at 2 players a few times and it was nevertheless enjoyable. Seems like the Erie & Narrow Gauge expansion can help make both 2 and 5 player games a little more balanced.

I was effectively shut out of the game before the second full round of play.  A really terrible experience.  I really hope that this is because there were five players, and that somebody has to get screwed with that number, because getting screwed out of the game through no fault of my own is a horrible design flaw.

RATING: 7
1 play so far
Seems like a solid stock game. Relatively "pure" stock interaction/negotiation with a few different goals possible for the companies/railroads. 

The highlight for me was the limited action pool and the dynamic that created with having to predict upcoming player's turns to see what's most beneficial for you. 

Downsides for me were the amount of math required to figure out incomes. It's a double-edged sword since that obfuscates winners and losers. It could be fairly AP prone because of this though. (Plus I'm just bad at math... yes even simple halving and addition)

Since stock games aren't my particular cup of tea, I don't think I'll ever love the game, but it's solid enough that I'd play it as a "token" stocks game occasionally.

RATING: 7
Wow.  Just ... WOW.  What a tight economical game.  There is a lot to weigh and manage during the game, and given the perfect information of it all, there's a good chance that it will take you a game to understand the ways that everything relates.  But since the game only lasts about an hour, so what?  Play it once to get a feel for it, and then play it again immediately afterward.  About halfway through our first game, I saw what I SHOULD have been doing, and saw how we couldn't catch up to the person in the lead.  By the second game, I thought I was out of the running when someone pointed out that I could dilute someone else's stock, and then I realized that I had a different option that took me to the win.  That third game was an out-and-out beat down, though....  So far, it's a unique experience.

That's my rating for Wabash Cannonball, the exact same game as this one.  I knocked this printing down a point because the artwork is far busier than it needs to be, and it makes it much harder for me to be able to read the board.

RATING: N/A
Harry Wu's Chicago Express is an innovative new boardgame with no luck factor. Chicago Express was originally released in a limited format by Winsome Games as Wabash Cannonball and it was the first game in Winsome's Historic Railroads System.
\13\\13\
In the game the B&O, C&O, Pennsylvania and New York Central railroads drive from the East Coast across the growing eastern US to Chicago. Smaller, more aggressive railroads like the Wabash spring up to further expand America's extensive railroads. The sharpest railroad executives vie for the maximum return on their investment in this business game lasting about one hour. This game can be played by 2-6 players, but it is best with 3 or 4 players. Ages 14 and up
\13\\13\
Reimplements: Wabash Cannonball

RATING: N/A
A straight bidding card game to claim the highest total from pairs of random valued point tiles. Each player has an identical deck of bid cards. Hand management to pace and ration your high value cards through the auctions. The trick is when you claim a tile, its value counts against your bid value for bidding on the second tile in the pair.

RATING: N/A
8 after 1 play

The auctioning of shares and payment of dividends mechanics work well together to create an intriguing game. Thematically, however, the payment of dividends from the bank does not make sense, but is required for the game mechanic to work. Player interaction is high, driven by the auction and competition for spaces on the board to build train routes. 

Artwork and game components are poor. The choice of colour and hue for the train companies make it a little difficult to tell them apart. The use of paper money also affects the game experience, especially the repeated checking of how much money each train company still has for laying tracks.

RATING: 7.5
A surprisingly deep and opaque economic game you can play in about an hour... I have no idea how this flew under my radar so long.  Buy stocks in companies and use that investment to grow the companies and increase their dividends.  Finding the most competitive companies and getting the best shares in them seems to be key... but there is a lot going on that make the game space very intriguing to explore.  I'm looking forward to more plays soon.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Ok, the game is truly ugly, literally.  This is my first exposure to a Winsome product (besides AoS, if that counts), so it may not be a surprise to others, but I was a bit taken back by the quality of the components.  It won't win any presentation awards that is for sure.

So how does it play?  All of those high ratings certainly raised my expectation by quite a lot.  True to John's words Wabash Cannonball is completely devoid of luck, there isn't a single random factor to be found, except for the actions your opponents will take.  Even though I'm not a big fan of luck based mechanics, but I do have to admit their inclusion, whether through card or cube drawing, does make each play somewhat different from each other, thereby contributing to the replayability of a game, as well as the need to modify your strategy.  So I'm not entirely sure how Wabash Cannonball will stand up to repeated plays.  The four competing corporations that you begin with in the game start their operation at the same end of the board (the eastern coast), pretty much right next to each other, and I imagine every time it is a race to see who can build out to the west faster.  Although I'd be interested in seeing if anyone will ever take a different approach by extending the tracks in a north-south direction, but somehow I am dubious that it will happen very often since it doesn't seem very profitable.

There were also a couple of gameplay issues that we encountered.  First, when the Wabash Cannonball Corporation finally operated the game was dangerously close to ending (2 of the existing companies already sold out of their stocks), so we did not really understand why would anyone want to invest in it if there's a high possibility that Wabash may not pay off (in our case we decided not to be mean to the person that bought the first share by ending the game right afterwards, so he did actually profited from the purchase eventually).  Also, I myself felt like I was behind the entire game.  I started out with less shares than everyone else, owning no majority in any one corporation, so I have to devise ways to make them spend their money (which would have the effect of giving them more certificates and thus more income) in order to have a chance to outbid them later, but even that was difficult because my earning power is so much less.  Surprisingly I came in second in the end, but I don't know if that was purely coincidental, or to attribute it to the brilliance of the game design, which may have avoided the pitfall of the rich getting richer.

With that said.  I enjoyed the game and would definitely want to play again soon.  I'm just not quite certain if Wabash Cannonball is the real deal like others claimed it to be, or it is merely a fluke.  Only time will tell.

Update #1: An adjustment of 0.5 rating downward after playing Pampas.  While Wabash is definitely harder and has more meat to it, I found myself having more fun with the former.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Upgraded to a 10. This game is awesome. This just ain't my type of game, and I still like it a lot because what's happening in the game is so intriguing.

Players buy shares in railroad companies as they stretch out from the east coast to the west coast, and it's all a question of self-balancing prices, calculating your stake and see to that you get a little of everything. You need others' help to get the company to grow, yet you still want the majority of the shares, right?
